</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Vaishnavism" title="Template:Vaishnavism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Vaishnavism" title="Template talk:Vaishnavism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Vaishnavism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i><b>Bhagavad Gita</b></i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">श्रीमद्भगवद्गीता</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Sanskrit" title="Romanization of Sanskrit">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">śrīmadbhagavadgītā</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'The Song by God';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[a]</a></sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20142_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20142-2">[1]</a></sup> often referred to as the <i><b>Gita</b></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAST" title="IAST">IAST</a>: <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">gītā</i></span></i>), is a 700-<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_prosody" title="Sanskrit prosody">verse</a> <a href="/wiki/Hindu_texts" title="Hindu texts">Hindu scripture</a> that is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_epic" title="Hindu epic">epic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> (chapters 23–40 of book 6 of the Mahabharata called the <a href="/wiki/Bhishma_Parva" title="Bhishma Parva">Bhishma Parva</a>), dated to the second half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_millennium_BCE" title="First millennium BCE">first millennium BCE</a> and is typical of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_synthesis" title="Hindu synthesis">Hindu synthesis</a>. It is considered to be one of the holy scriptures for <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.
</p><p>The <i>Gita</i> is set in a narrative framework of a dialogue between <a href="/wiki/Pandava" title="Pandava">Pandava</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Arjuna" title="Arjuna">Arjuna</a> and his guide and charioteer <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>, the Personality of Godhead. At the start of the <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a> <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">Yuddha</a> (righteous war) between <a href="/wiki/Pandava" title="Pandava">Pandavas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaurava" title="Kaurava">Kauravas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arjuna" title="Arjuna">Arjuna</a> is preoccupied by a <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral</a> and emotional <a href="/wiki/Dilemma" title="Dilemma">dilemma</a> and despair about the violence and death the war will cause in the battle against his kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> Wondering if he should renounce the war, he seeks Krishna's counsel, whose answers and discourse constitute the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>. Krishna counsels Arjuna to "fulfil his <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> (warrior) duty to uphold the <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>" through Karma "selfless action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[note 1]</a></sup> The Krishna–Arjuna dialogues cover a broad range of spiritual topics, touching upon ethical dilemmas and philosophical issues that go far beyond the war, Arjuna faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20142_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20142-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200460–62_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200460–62-7">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii-8">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous commentaries have been written on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> with widely differing views on the essentials. According to some, the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> was written by the god <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a>, as told to him by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda_Vyasa" title="Veda Vyasa">Veda Vyasa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> commentators read varying relationships between Self and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> in the text: <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> sees the non-dualism of <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Atman</a> (Self) and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> (universal Self) as its essence';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutsch_2004_page=97_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deutsch_2004_page=97-9">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bhedabheda" title="Bhedabheda">Bhedabheda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vishishtadvaita" title="Vishishtadvaita">Vishishtadvaita</a> see Atman and Brahman as both different and not different; while <a href="/wiki/Dvaita_Vedanta" title="Dvaita Vedanta">Dvaita Vedanta</a> sees the dualism of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">Atman</a> (Self) and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> as its essence. The setting of the <i>Gita</i> in a battlefield has been interpreted as an allegory for the ethical and moral struggles of human life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii-8">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201615_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201615-11">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> presents a synthesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461-12">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000-13">[10]</a></sup> of Hindu ideas about <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461-12">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">bhakti</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">yogic</a> ideals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000-13">[10]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000-13">[10]</a></sup> The text covers <a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">Jñāna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">Bhakti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">Karma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81ja_yoga" title="Rāja yoga">Rāja yogas</a> (spoken of in the 6th chapter),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup> incorporating ideas from the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a>-<a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a> philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[note 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is the most revered of all the Hindu texts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson2014viii_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson2014viii-17">[13]</a></sup> and has a unique pan-Hindu influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buitenen7_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-buitenen7-18">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neusner2009_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neusner2009-19">[15]</a></sup> The <i>Gita</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s call for selfless action inspired many leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence movement</a> including <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Bal Gangadhar Tilak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a>, the latter referring to it as his "spiritual dictionary".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> in the title of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> means "song." Religious leaders and scholars interpret the word <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhagav%C4%81n" title="Bhagavān">Bhagavad</a></i> in a number of ways. Accordingly, the title has been interpreted as "the word of God" by the theistic schools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> "the words of the Lord",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup> "the Divine Song",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> and "Celestial Song" by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> 
In India, its Sanskrit name is often written as Shrimad Bhagavad Gita, श्रीमद् भगवद् गीता (the latter two words often written as a single word भगवद्गीता), where the Shrimad prefix is used to denote a high degree of respect. This is not to be confused with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrimad_Bhagavatam" title="Shrimad Bhagavatam">Shrimad Bhagavatam</a>, which is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purana" title="Purana">Purana</a> dealing with the life of the Hindu God <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> and various avatars of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>.
</p><p>The work is also known as the <i>Iswara Gita</i>, the <i>Ananta Gita</i>, the <i>Hari Gita</i>, the <i>Vyasa Gita</i>, or simply the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986ix_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986ix-26">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authorship">Authorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Authorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vyasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1311" data-file-width="1023" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vyasa.jpg/220px-Vyasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vyasa.jpg/330px-Vyasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Vyasa.jpg/440px-Vyasa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vyasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Bhagavata Gita</i> is attributed to the sage <a href="/wiki/Vyasa" title="Vyasa">Vyasa</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the Indian tradition, the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, as well as the epic <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> of which it is a part, is attributed to the sage <a href="/wiki/Vyasa" title="Vyasa">Vyasa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Folwerxxvi_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Folwerxxvi-27">[23]</a></sup> whose full name was Krishna Dvaipayana, also called Veda-Vyasa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201616_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201616-28">[24]</a></sup> Another Hindu legend states that Vyasa narrated it while the lord <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> broke one of his tusks and wrote down the Mahabharata along with the Bhagavad Gita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p168_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p168-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavitt2015vii_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slavitt2015vii-30">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[note 3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars consider Vyasa to be a mythical or symbolic author, in part because Vyasa is also the traditional compiler of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, texts dated to be from different millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p168_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p168-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201437,_Quote:_&quot;Textual_historians_generally_prefer_terms_that_undercut_any_implications_of_Vyasa's_actual_authorship._They_refer_to_Vyasa_as_a_mythical_or_symbolic_author_of_the_Mahabharata.&quot;_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201437,_Quote:_&quot;Textual_historians_generally_prefer_terms_that_undercut_any_implications_of_Vyasa's_actual_authorship._They_refer_to_Vyasa_as_a_mythical_or_symbolic_author_of_the_Mahabharata.&quot;-35">[30]</a></sup> The word <i>Vyasa</i> literally means "arranger, compiler", and is a surname in India. According to Kashi Nath Upadhyaya, a <i>Gita</i> scholar, it is possible that a number of different individuals with the same name compiled different texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199825_with_footnote_1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199825_with_footnote_1-36">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>, the 19th-century Hindu monk and Vedantist, stated that the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> may be old but it was mostly unknown in the Indian history till early 8th century when <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> (Shankaracharya) made it famous by writing his much-followed commentary on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vivekanandabg_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-vivekanandabg-37">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> Some infer, states Vivekananda, that "Shankaracharya was the author of <i>Gita</i>, and that it was he who foisted it into the body of the <i>Mahabharata</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vivekanandabg_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-vivekanandabg-37">[32]</a></sup> This attribution to Adi Shankara is unlikely in part because Shankara himself refers to the earlier commentaries on the Bhagavad Gita, and because other Hindu texts and traditions that compete with the ideas of Shankara refer to much older literature referencing the Bhagavad Gita, though much of this ancient secondary literature has not survived into the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vivekanandabg_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-vivekanandabg-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/J._A._B._van_Buitenen" title="J. A. B. van Buitenen">J. A. B. van Buitenen</a>, an Indologist known for his translations and scholarship on <i>Mahabharata</i>, the <i>Gita</i> is so contextually and philosophically well knit with the <i>Mahabharata</i> that it was not an independent text that "somehow wandered into the epic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buitenen2013p5_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-buitenen2013p5-39">[34]</a></sup> The Gita, states van Buitenen, was conceived and developed by the <i>Mahabharata</i> authors to "bring to a climax and solution the dharmic dilemma of a war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buitenen2013p5_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-buitenen2013p5-39">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alexus_McLeod&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alexus McLeod (page does not exist)">Alexus McLeod</a>, a scholar of Philosophy and Asian Studies, it is "impossible to link the Bhagavad Gita to a single author", and it may be the work of many authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2014p168_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2014p168-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982xxxiv,_Quote:_"Therefore,_instead_of_the_traditional_view_of_authorship,_many_scholars_have_argued_that_the_Gita_is_not_the_work_of_one_author_but_a_composite_work."_43-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982xxxiv,_Quote:_"Therefore,_instead_of_the_traditional_view_of_authorship,_many_scholars_have_argued_that_the_Gita_is_not_the_work_of_one_author_but_a_composite_work."-43'>[37]</a></sup> This view is shared by the Indologist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Llewellyn_Basham" title="Arthur Llewellyn Basham">Arthur Basham</a>, who states that there were three or more authors or compilers of Bhagavad Gita. This is evidenced by the discontinuous intermixing of philosophical verses with theistic or passionately theistic verses, according to Basham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p85_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p85-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[note 5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some people suggest during the era of <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">colonial Hindustan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_Hastings" title="Warren Hastings">Warren Hastings</a> separated out <b>25th</b> to <b>42nd</b> chapters of <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a> and published total 18 chapters under the name of <b>Dialogues of Kreeshna and Arjoon in Eighteen lectures with Notes</b> from <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Before this edition of 18th century there was no separate book named after <b>Geeta</b> during <b>Punthi</b> era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Date">Date</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Date">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Theories on the date of the composition of the <i>Gita</i> vary considerably. Some scholars accept dates from the fifth century to the second century BCE as the probable range, the latter likely. The Hinduism scholar Jeaneane Fowler, in her commentary on the Gita, considers second century BCE to be the probable date of composition.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv-47">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._A._B._van_Buitenen" title="J. A. B. van Buitenen">J. A. B. van Buitenen</a> also states that the Gita was likely composed about 200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen20136,_Quote:_"ca._200_BC_is_a_likely_date"._48-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen20136,_Quote:_"ca._200_BC_is_a_likely_date".-48'>[41]</a></sup> According to the Indologist <a href="/wiki/Arvind_Sharma" title="Arvind Sharma">Arvind Sharma</a>, the <i>Gita</i> is generally accepted to be a 2nd-century-BCE text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma19863_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma19863-49">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kurukshetra.jpg"><img alt="An old torn paper with a painting depicting the Mahabharata war, with some verses recorded in Sanskrit." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="614" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg/260px-Kurukshetra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg/390px-Kurukshetra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg/520px-Kurukshetra.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kurukshetra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A manuscript illustration of the battle of Kurukshetra, fought between the Kauravas and the Pandavas, recorded in the <i>Mahabharata</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Kashi Nath Upadhyaya, in contrast, dates it a bit earlier. He states that the Gita was always a part of the <i>Mahabharata</i>, and dating the latter suffices in dating the Gita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18-50">[43]</a></sup> On the basis of the estimated dates of <i>Mahabharata</i> as evidenced by exact quotes of it in the Buddhist literature by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asvaghosa" title="Asvaghosa">Asvaghosa</a> (c. 100 CE), Upadhyaya states that the <i>Mahabharata</i>, and therefore <i>Gita</i>, must have been well known by then for a Buddhist to be quoting it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18-50">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[note 6]</a></sup> This suggests a <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terminus_ante_quem" title="wikt:terminus ante quem">terminus ante quem</a></i> (latest date) of the <i>Gita</i> to be sometime prior to the 1st century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199816–18-50">[43]</a></sup> He cites similar quotes in the Dharmasutra texts, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_sutras" title="Brahma sutras">Brahma sutras</a></i>, and other literature to conclude that the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> was composed in the fifth or fourth century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199817–19_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199817–19-53">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Arthur Basham, the context of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> suggests that it was composed in an era when the <a href="/wiki/Dharma-yuddha" title="Dharma-yuddha">ethics of war</a> were being questioned and renunciation to monastic life was becoming popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p95_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p95-56">[47]</a></sup> Such an era emerged after the rise of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> in the 5th century BCE, and particularly after the semi-legendary life of <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> in 3rd century BCE. Thus, the first version of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> may have been composed in or after the 3rd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p95_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p95-56">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguistically, the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit#Classical_Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">classical Sanskrit</a> of the early variety, states the <i>Gita</i> scholar <a href="/wiki/Winthrop_Sargeant" title="Winthrop Sargeant">Winthrop Sargeant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4-57">[48]</a></sup> The text has occasional pre-classical elements of the Sanskrit language, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">aorist</a> and the prohibitive <i>mā</i> instead of the expected <i>na</i> (not) of classical Sanskrit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4-57">[48]</a></sup> This suggests that the text was composed after the <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> era, but before the long compounds of classical Sanskrit became the norm. This would date the text as transmitted by the <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a> to the later centuries of the 1st-millennium BCE, and the first written version probably to the 2nd or 3rd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20093–4-57">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Jeaneane Fowler, "the dating of the <i>Gita</i> varies considerably" and depends in part on whether one accepts it to be a part of the early versions of the <i>Mahabharata</i>, or a text that was inserted into the epic at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv–xxiv_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv–xxiv-59">[50]</a></sup> The earliest "surviving" components therefore are believed to be no older than the earliest "external" references we have to the <i>Mahabharata</i> epic. The <i>Mahabharata</i> – the world's longest poem – is itself a text that was likely written and compiled over several hundred years, one dated between "400 BCE or little earlier, and 2nd century CE, though some claim a few parts can be put as late as 400 CE", states Fowler. The dating of the <i>Gita</i> is thus dependent on the uncertain dating of the <i>Mahabharata</i>. The actual dates of composition of the <i>Gita</i> remain unresolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv–xxiv_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxiv–xxiv-59">[50]</a></sup> While the year and century is uncertain, states <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_Davis_(professor_of_religion)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Richard Davis (professor of religion) (page does not exist)">Richard Davis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[51]</a></sup> the internal evidence in the text dates the origin of the <i>Gita</i> discourse to the Hindu lunar month of <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Margashirsha" title="wikt:Margashirsha">Margashirsha</a></i> (also called <i><a href="/wiki/Agrahayana" title="Agrahayana">Agrahayana</a></i>, generally December or January of the Gregorian calendar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20143_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20143-61">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_and_significance">Composition and significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Composition and significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is the best known,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[53]</a></sup> and most influential of Hindu scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson2014viii_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson2014viii-17">[13]</a></sup> While Hinduism is known for its diversity and its synthesis therefrom, the Bhagavad Gita has a unique pan-Hindu influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buitenen7_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-buitenen7-18">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maitra2018_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maitra2018-63">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerald_James_Larson" title="Gerald James Larson">Gerald James Larson</a> – an Indologist and classical Hindu Philosophies scholar, states "if there is any one text that comes near to embodying the totality of what it is to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>, it would be the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson2014viii_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson2014viii-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neusner2009_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neusner2009-19">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Prasthanatrayi" title="Prasthanatrayi">Prasthanatrayi</a>, which also includes the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_sutras" title="Brahma sutras">Brahma sutras</a>. These are the three starting points for the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> school of Hindu philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nvisaeva35_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-nvisaeva35-64">[55]</a></sup> The Brahma sutras constitute the <i>Nyāya prasthāna</i> or the "starting point of reasoning canonical base", while the principal Upanishads constitute the <i>Sruti prasthāna</i> or the "starting point of heard scriptures", and the Bhagavad Gita constitutes the <i>Smriti prasthāna</i> or the "starting point of remembered canonical base".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nvisaeva35_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-nvisaeva35-64">[55]</a></sup> The Bhagavad Gita is a "summation of the Vedanta", states Sargeant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x-65">[56]</a></sup> It is thus one of the key texts for the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson20107_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson20107-66">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh200537_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh200537-67">[58]</a></sup> a school that provides one of the theoretical foundations for Hinduism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura19503_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura19503-68">[59]</a></sup> and one that has had an enormous influence over time, becoming the central ideology of the Hindu renaissance in the 19th century, according to <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Flood" title="Gavin Flood">Gavin Flood</a> – a scholar of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231–232,_238_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231–232,_238-69">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna,_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="304" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna%2C_crop.jpg/150px-Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna%2C_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna%2C_crop.jpg/225px-Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna%2C_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna%2C_crop.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arjuna_and_His_Charioteer_Krishna_Confront_Karna,_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Krishna recounts <i>Gita</i> to Arjuna during <a href="/wiki/Kurukshetra_War" title="Kurukshetra War">Kurukshetra War</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>; c.1820 painting.</div></div></div>
<p>Some Hindus give it the status of an Upanishad, and some consider it to be a "revealed text".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[63]</a></sup> Others consider the Bhagavad Gita as an important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smrti" title="Smrti">Smriti</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[64]</a></sup> or secondary text that exist in alternate versions such as one found in Kashmir though it does not affect the basic message of the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11-74">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201439–40_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201439–40-75">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982li–lii,_Quote:_"the_Kashmir_recension_is_a_later_reading_of_the_Gita."_(note_the_different_views_of_F_Otto_Schrader_from_those_of_SK_Belvalkar_as_well_as_JAB_van_Buitenen.)_76-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982li–lii,_Quote:_"the_Kashmir_recension_is_a_later_reading_of_the_Gita."_(note_the_different_views_of_F_Otto_Schrader_from_those_of_SK_Belvalkar_as_well_as_JAB_van_Buitenen.)-76'>[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu_synthesis">Hindu synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hindu synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Smarta_tradition" title="Smarta tradition">Smarta tradition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg/220px-A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg/330px-A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg/440px-A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_didactic_print_from_the_1960%27s_that_uses_the_Gita_scene_as_a_focal_point_for_general_religious_instruction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A didactic print from the 1960s that uses the Gita scene as a focal point for general religious instruction</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is the sealing achievement of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_synthesis" title="Hindu synthesis">Hindu synthesis</a>, incorporating its various religious traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000-13">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup> The synthesis is at both philosophical and socio-religious levels, states the <i>Gita</i> scholar Keya Maitra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra20185,_26–30,_143_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra20185,_26–30,_143-77">[68]</a></sup> The text refrains from insisting on one right <i>marga</i> (path) to spirituality. It openly synthesizes and inclusively accepts multiple ways of life, harmonizing spiritual pursuits through action (<i>karma</i>), knowledge (<i>gyaana</i>), and devotion (<i>bhakti</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson200669–70,_95–100_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson200669–70,_95–100-78">[69]</a></sup> According to the <i>Gita</i> translator Radhakrishnan, quoted in a review by Robinson, Krishna's discourse is a "comprehensive synthesis" that inclusively unifies the competing strands of Hindu thought such as "Vedic ritual, Upanishadic wisdom, devotional theism and philosophical insight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson200695_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson200695-79">[70]</a></sup> Aurobindo described the text as a synthesis of various <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yogas</a>. The Indologist Robert Minor, and others,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup> in contrast, state the <i>Gita</i> is "more clearly defined as a synthesis of Vedanta, Yoga and Samkhya" philosophies of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198674–75,_81_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198674–75,_81-80">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The synthesis in <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> addresses the question as to what constitutes the virtuous path and one necessary for the spiritual liberation and a release from the cycles of rebirth (<i>moksha</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood2004p85_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood2004p85-81">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill2017p129_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill2017p129-82">[73]</a></sup> It discusses whether one should renounce a householder lifestyle for a life as an ascetic, or lead a householder life dedicated to one's duty and profession, or pursue a householder life devoted to a personalized god in the revealed form of Krishna. Thus <i>Gita</i> discusses and synthesizes the three dominant trends in Hinduism: enlightenment-based renunciation, dharma-based householder life, and devotion-based theism. According to Deutsch and Dalvi, the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> attempts "to forge a harmony" between these three paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[note 8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s synthetic answer recommends that one must resist the "either-or" view, and consider a "both-and" view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zaehner1973p187_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zaehner1973p187-84">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[76]</a></sup> It states the dharmic householder can achieve the same goals as the renouncing monk through "inner renunciation", that is "motiveless action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood2004p85_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flood2004p85-81">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[note 9]</a></sup> One must do the right thing because one has determined that it is right, states <i>Gita</i>, without craving for its fruits, without worrying about the results, loss or gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Framarin_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Framarin-88">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basham1991p86_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basham1991p86-90">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[80]</a></sup> Desires, selfishness and the craving for fruits can distort one from the dharmic action and spiritual living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basham1991p86_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Basham1991p86-90">[79]</a></sup> The <i>Gita</i> synthesis goes further, according to its interpreters such as Swami Vivekananda, and the text states that there is Living God in every human being and the devoted service to this Living God in everyone – without craving for personal rewards – is a means to spiritual development and liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p82_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p82-92">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198638–39,_123–128,_143_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198638–39,_123–128,_143-94">[83]</a></sup> According to Galvin Flood, the teachings in <i>Gita</i> differ from other Indian religions that encouraged extreme austerity and self-torture of various forms (<i>karsayanta</i>). The <i>Gita</i> disapproves of these, stating that not only is it against the tradition but against Krishna himself, because "Krishna dwells within all beings, in torturing the body the ascetic would be torturing him", states Flood. Even a monk should strive for the "inner renunciation", rather than external pretensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Gita</i> synthesizes several paths to spiritual realization based on the premise that people are born with different temperaments and tendencies (<i>guna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii-96">[85]</a></sup> According to Winthrop Sargeant, the text acknowledges that some individuals are more reflective and intellectual, some affective and engaged by their emotions, some are action driven, yet others favor experimenting and exploring what works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii-96">[85]</a></sup> It then presents different spiritual paths for each personality type respectively: the path of knowledge (<i>jnana yoga</i>), the path of devotion (<i>bhakti yoga</i>), the path of action (<i>karma yoga</i>), and the path of meditation (<i>raja yoga</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii-96">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson200692–93,_133–134_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson200692–93,_133–134-97">[86]</a></sup> The <i>guna</i> premise is a synthesis of the ideas from the Samkhya school of Hinduism. According to Upadhyaya, the <i>Gita</i> states that none of these paths to spiritual realization is "intrinsically superior or inferior", rather they "converge in one and lead to the same goal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya1998474–475_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya1998474–475-98">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Hiltebeitel, <i>Bhakti</i> forms an essential ingredient of this synthesis, and the text incorporates <i>Bhakti</i> into <i>Vedanta</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel2002_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel2002-99">[88]</a></sup> According to Scheepers, The Bhagavad Gita is a Brahmanical text which uses the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shramana" title="Shramana">shramanic</a> and Yogic terminology to spread the Brahmanic idea of living according to one's duty or <i>dharma</i>, in contrast to the ascetic ideal of liberation by avoiding all karma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000122–127_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheepers2000122–127-100">[89]</a></sup> According to Galvin Flood and Charles Martin, the <i>Gita</i> rejects the shramanic path of non-action, emphasizing instead "the renunciation of the fruits of action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[90]</a></sup> The Bhagavad Gita, states Raju, is a great synthesis of the ideas of the impersonal spiritual monism with personal God, of "the <i>yoga</i> of action with the <i>yoga</i> of transcendence of action, and these again with <i>yogas</i> of devotion and knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscripts">Manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavad_Gita,_a_19th_century_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of four pieces of paper with verses in Sanskrit." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="389" decoding="async" height="428" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Bhagavad_Gita%2C_a_19th_century_manuscript.jpg/260px-Bhagavad_Gita%2C_a_19th_century_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/Bhagavad_Gita%2C_a_19th_century_manuscript.jpg 1.5x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavad_Gita,_a_19th_century_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 19th-century Sanskrit manuscript of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, Devanagari script</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> manuscript is found in the sixth book of the <i>Mahabharata</i> manuscripts – the <i>Bhisma-parvan</i>. Therein, in the third section, the <i>Gita</i> forms chapters 23–40, that is 6.3.23 to 6.3.40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxi–xxii_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxi–xxii-102">[91]</a></sup> The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is often preserved and studied on its own, as an independent text with its chapters renumbered from 1 to 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxi–xxii_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxi–xxii-102">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="939" data-file-width="1354" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg/220px-Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg/330px-Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg/440px-Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vintage_Hindu_God_Krishan_Gita_Birth_Litho_Print_Original_Vasudeo_Pandya_1932.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vintage Hindu God Krishan Gita Birth Litho Print Original Vasudeo Pandya 1932</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> manuscripts exist in numerous Indic scripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nadkarni18_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-nadkarni18-103">[92]</a></sup> These include writing systems that are currently in use, as well as early scripts such as the <a href="/wiki/Sharada_script" title="Sharada script">Sharada script</a>, now dormant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nadkarni18_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-nadkarni18-103">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[93]</a></sup> Variant manuscripts of the <i>Gita</i> have been found on the Indian subcontinent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11-74">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-minor1982lli-105">[94]</a></sup> Unlike the enormous variations in the remaining sections of the surviving <i>Mahabharata</i> manuscripts, the <i>Gita</i> manuscripts show only minor variations and the meaning is the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpadhyaya199810–12_with_footnote_1_on_p._11-74">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-minor1982lli-105">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Gambhirananda, the old manuscripts may have had 745 verses, though he agrees that “700 verses is the generally accepted historic standard."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGambhirananda1997xvii_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGambhirananda1997xvii-106">[95]</a></sup> Gambhirananda's view is supported by a few versions of chapter 6.43 of the <i>Mahabharata</i>. These versions state the <i>Gita</i> is a text where "Kesava [Krishna] spoke 574 slokas, Arjuna 84, Sanjaya 41, and Dhritarashtra 1", states the Religious Studies and <i>Gita</i> exegesis scholar Robert Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–li_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–li-107">[96]</a></sup> An authentic manuscript of the <i>Gita</i> with 745 verses has not been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–ii_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–ii-108">[97]</a></sup> Adi Shankara, in his 8th-century commentary, explicitly states that the Gita has 700 verses, which was likely a deliberate declaration to prevent further insertions and changes to the Gita. Since Shankara's time, the "700 verses" has been the standard benchmark for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_edition" title="Critical edition">critical edition</a> of the Bhagavad Gita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–ii_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982l–ii-108">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Content">Content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Content">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is a poem written in the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood2013xviii_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-flood2013xviii-109">[98]</a></sup> Its 700 <a href="/wiki/Verse_(poetry)" title="Verse (poetry)">verses</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-minor1982lli-105">[94]</a></sup> are structured into several ancient Indian poetic <a href="/wiki/Metre_(poetry)" title="Metre (poetry)">meters</a>, with the principal being the <i>shloka</i> (<i>Anushtubh chanda</i>). It has 18 chapters in total. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoburn199127_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoburn199127-110">[99]</a></sup> Each <i>shloka</i> consists of a couplet, thus the entire text consists of 1,400 lines. Each <i>shloka</i> line has two quarter verses with exactly eight syllables. Each of these quarters is further arranged into "two metrical feet of four syllables each", state Flood and Martin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood2013xviii_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-flood2013xviii-109">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[note 10]</a></sup> The metered verse does not rhyme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20098_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20098-111">[100]</a></sup> While the <i>shloka</i> is the principal meter in the <i>Gita</i>, it does deploy other elements of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_prosody" title="Sanskrit prosody">Sanskrit prosody</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEgenes20034_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEgenes20034-113">[101]</a></sup> At dramatic moments, it uses the <i>tristubh</i> meter found in the Vedas, where each line of the couplet has two quarter verses with exactly eleven syllables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20098_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20098-111">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrative">Narrative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Narrative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> is a dialogue between Krishna and Arjuna right before the start of the climactic <a href="/wiki/Kurukshetra_War" title="Kurukshetra War">Kurukshetra War</a> in the Hindu epic <i>Mahabharata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[note 11]</a></sup> Two massive armies have gathered to destroy the other. The Pandava prince Arjuna asks his charioteer <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> to drive to the center of the battlefield so that he can get a good look at both the armies and all those "so eager for war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2-117">[104]</a></sup> He sees that some among his enemies are his own relatives, beloved friends, and revered teachers. He does not want to fight to kill them and is thus filled with doubt and despair on the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[105]</a></sup> He drops his bow, wonders if he should renounce and just leave the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2-117">[104]</a></sup> He turns to his charioteer and guide Krishna, for advice on the rationale for war, his choices and the right thing to do. The Bhagavad Gita is the compilation of Arjuna's questions and moral dilemma, Krishna's answers and insights that elaborate on a variety of philosophical concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2-117">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200459–61_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200459–61-119">[106]</a></sup> The compiled dialogue goes far beyond the "a rationale for war"; it touches on many human ethical dilemmas, philosophical issues and life's choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis20141–2-117">[104]</a></sup> According to Flood and Martin, although the <i>Gita</i> is set in the context of a war epic, the narrative is structured to apply to all situations; it wrestles with questions about "who we are, how we should live our lives, and how should we act in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[107]</a></sup> According to Sargeant, it delves into questions about the "purpose of life, crisis of self-identity, human Self, human temperaments, and ways for spiritual quest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x–xviii-8">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Characters">Characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1610" data-file-width="2268" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg/260px-Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg/390px-Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg/520px-Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arjuna_Wijaya_chariot_statue_in_Jakarta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The thematic story of Arjuna and Krishna at the <a href="/wiki/Kurukshetra_War" title="Kurukshetra War">Kurukshetra War</a> became popular in southeast Asia as Hinduism spread there in the 1st-millennium CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[109]</a></sup> Above, an Arjuna-Krishna chariot scene in Jakarta center, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arjuna" title="Arjuna">Arjuna</a>, one of the five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandavas" title="Pandavas">Pandavas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>, Arjuna's charioteer and <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a> who was actually an incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sanjaya" title="Sanjaya">Sanjaya</a>, counselor of the Kuru king <a href="/wiki/Dhritarashtra" title="Dhritarashtra">Dhritarashtra</a> (secondary narrator)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dhritarashtra" title="Dhritarashtra">Dhritarashtra</a>, Kuru king (Sanjaya's audience) and father of the Kauravas</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapters">Chapters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chapters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Bhagavad Gita</i> comprises 18 chapters (section 23 to 40)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[web 2]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Bhishma_Parva" title="Bhishma Parva">Bhishma Parva</a> of the epic <i>Mahabharata</i>. Because of differences in <a href="/wiki/Recension" title="Recension">recensions</a>, the verses of the <i>Gita</i> may be numbered in the full text of the <i>Mahabharata</i> as chapters 6.25–42 or as chapters 6.23–40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhandarkar_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhandarkar-125">[web 3]</a></sup> The number of verses in each chapter vary in some manuscripts of the <i>Gita</i> discovered on the Indian subcontinent. However, variant readings are relatively few in contrast to the numerous versions of the <i>Mahabharata</i> it is found embedded in, and the meaning is the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-minor1982lli-105">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> has no chapter titles. Some Sanskrit editions that separate the <i>Gita</i> from the epic as an independent text, as well as translators, however, add chapter titles such as each chapter being a particular form of yoga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra201839_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra201839-126">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhandarkar_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bhandarkar-125">[web 3]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Swami_Chidbhavananda" title="Swami Chidbhavananda">Swami Chidbhavananda</a> describes each of the eighteen chapters as a separate yoga because each chapter, like yoga, "trains the body and the mind". He labels the first chapter "Arjuna Vishada Yogam" or the "Yoga of Arjuna's Dejection".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[112]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Arnold" title="Edwin Arnold">Edwin Arnold</a> titled this chapter in his 1885 translation as "The Distress of Arjuna".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[note 12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_1:_Arjuna_Vishadayoga_.2847_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_1:_Arjuna_Vishadayoga_(47_verses)">Chapter 1: Arjuna Vishadayoga (47 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Chapter 1: Arjuna Vishadayoga (47 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators have variously titled the first chapter as <i>Arjuna vishada yoga</i>, <i>Prathama Adhyaya</i>, <i>The Distress of Arjuna</i>, <i>The War Within</i>, or <i>Arjuna's Sorrow</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The Bhagavad Gita is opened by setting the stage of the Kurukshetra battlefield. Two massive armies representing different loyalties and ideologies face a catastrophic war. With Arjuna is Krishna, not as a participant in the war, but only as his charioteer and counsel. Arjuna requests Krishna to move the <a href="/wiki/Ratha" title="Ratha">chariot</a> between the two armies so he can see those "eager for this war". He sees family and friends on the enemy side. Arjuna is distressed and in sorrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200771–82_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200771–82-133">[117]</a></sup> The issue is, states <a href="/wiki/Arvind_Sharma" title="Arvind Sharma">Arvind Sharma</a>, "is it morally proper to kill?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv-134">[118]</a></sup> This and other moral dilemmas in the first chapter are set in a context where the Hindu epic and Krishna have already extolled <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">ahimsa</a></i> (non-violence) to be the highest and divine virtue of a human being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv-134">[118]</a></sup> The war feels evil to Arjuna and he questions the morality of war. He wonders if it is noble to renounce and leave before the violence starts, or should he fight, and why.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200771–82_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200771–82-133">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_2:_Sankhyayoga_.2872_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_2:_Sankhyayoga_(72_verses)">Chapter 2: Sankhyayoga (72 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Chapter 2: Sankhyayoga (72 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
