2x" width="220"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Face pages of chapters 1, 2 and 3 of historic <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> manuscripts. Top: <a href="/wiki/Bengali_alphabet" title="Bengali alphabet">Bengali script</a>; Bottom: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurmukhi_script" title="Gurmukhi script">Gurmukhi script</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Sankhya Yoga</i>, <i>The Book of Doctrines</i>, <i>Self-Realization</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Knowledge (and Philosophy)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The second chapter begins the philosophical discussions and teachings found in <i>Gita</i>. The warrior Arjuna whose past had focused on learning the skills of his profession now faces a war he has doubts about. Filled with introspection and questions about the meaning and purpose of life, he asks Krishna about the nature of life, Self, death, afterlife and whether there is a deeper meaning and reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98-135">[119]</a></sup> Krishna answers. The chapter summarizes the Hindu idea of rebirth, samsara, eternal Self in each person (Self), universal Self present in everyone, various types of yoga, divinity within, the nature of Self-knowledge and other concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98-135">[119]</a></sup> The ideas and concepts in the second chapter reflect the framework of the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a> schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>. This chapter is an overview for the remaining sixteen chapters of the Bhagavad Gita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200783–98-135">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986xv–xvi_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986xv–xvi-136">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xx_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xx-137">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> memorized the last 19 verses of the second chapter, considering them as his companion in his non-violent movement for social justice during the colonial rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxviii_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxviii-138">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_3:_Karmayoga_.2843_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_3:_Karmayoga_(43_verses)">Chapter 3: Karmayoga (43 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Chapter 3: Karmayoga (43 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Karma yoga</i>, <i>Virtue in Work</i>, <i>Selfless Service</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Action</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> Arjuna, after listening to Krishna's spiritual teachings in Chapter 2, gets more confounded and returns to the predicament he faces. He wonders if fighting the war is "not so important after all" given Krishna's overview on the pursuit of spiritual wisdom. Krishna replies that there is no way to avoid action (karma), since abstention from work is also an action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110-139">[123]</a></sup> Krishna states that Arjuna has an obligation to understand and perform his duty (dharma), because everything is connected by the law of cause and effect. Every man or woman is bound by activity. Those who act selfishly create the karmic cause and are thereby bound to the effect which may be good or bad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110-139">[123]</a></sup> Those who act selflessly for the right cause and strive to do their dharmic duty do God's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110-139">[123]</a></sup> Those who act without craving for fruits are free from the karmic effects, because the results never motivated them. Whatever the result, it does not affect them. Their happiness comes from within, and the external world does not bother them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110_139-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200799–110-139">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler201250–63,_66–70_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler201250–63,_66–70-140">[124]</a></sup> According to Flood and Martin, chapter 3 and onwards develops "a theological response to Arjuna's dilemma".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_4:_Gyana_Karma_Sanyasayoga_.2842_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_4:_Gyana_Karma_Sanyasayoga_(42_verses)">Chapter 4: Gyana Karma Sanyasayoga (42 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Chapter 4: Gyana Karma Sanyasayoga (42 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the fourth chapter as <i>Jñāna–Karma-Sanyasa yoga</i>, <i>The Religion of Knowledge</i>, <i>Wisdom in Action</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Renunciation of Action through Knowledge</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> Krishna reveals that he has taught this yoga to the Vedic sages. Arjuna questions how Krishna could do this, when those sages lived so long ago, and Krishna was born more recently. Krishna reminds him that everyone is in the cycle of rebirths, and while Arjuna does not remember his previous births, he does. Whenever dharma declines and the purpose of life is forgotten by men, says Krishna, he returns to re-establish dharma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[note 13]</a></sup> Every time he returns, he teaches about inner Self in all beings. The later verses of the chapter return to the discussion of motiveless action and the need to determine the right action, performing it as one's dharma (duty) while renouncing the results, rewards, fruits. The simultaneous outer action with inner renunciation, states Krishna, is the secret to the life of freedom. Action leads to knowledge, while selfless action leads to spiritual awareness, state the last verses of this chapter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122-5">[3]</a></sup> The 4th chapter is the first time where Krishna begins to reveal his divine nature to Arjuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_5:_Karma_Sanyasayoga_.2829_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_5:_Karma_Sanyasayoga_(29_verses)">Chapter 5: Karma Sanyasayoga (29 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Chapter 5: Karma Sanyasayoga (29 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title this chapter as <i>Karma–Sanyasa yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Renouncing Fruits of Works</i>, <i>Renounce and Rejoice</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Renunciation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter starts by presenting the tension in the Indian tradition between the life of <i>sannyasa</i> (monks who have renounced their household and worldly attachments) and the life of <i>grihastha</i> (householder). Arjuna asks Krishna which path is better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller198659_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller198659-145">[128]</a></sup> Krishna answers that both are paths to the same goal, but the path of "selfless action and service" with inner renunciation is better. The different paths, says Krishna, aim for—and if properly pursued, lead to—Self-knowledge. This knowledge leads to the universal, transcendent Godhead, the divine essence in all beings, to Brahman – the Krishna himself. The final verses of the chapter state that the self-aware who have reached self-realization live without fear, anger, or desire. They are free within, always.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007123–132_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007123–132-146">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler201291–103_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler201291–103-147">[130]</a></sup> Chapter 5 shows signs of interpolations and internal contradictions. For example, states Arthur Basham, verses 5.23–28 state that a sage's spiritual goal is to realize the impersonal Brahman, yet the next verse 5.29 states that the goal is to realize the personal God who is Krishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p85_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p85-44">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="; background-color: #FFE0BB;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><b>Selfless service</b>
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6th_Chapter,_verse_1,_Bhagavad_Gita,_Sanskrit,_Devanagari_script.jpg"><img alt="6th Chapter, verse 1, Bhagavad Gita, Sanskrit, Devanagari script.jpg" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="1735" decoding="async" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg/260px-6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg/390px-6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg/520px-6th_Chapter%2C_verse_1%2C_Bhagavad_Gita%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Devanagari_script.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
</p>
<div class="poem">
<p>It is not those who lack energy<br/>
nor those who refrain from action,<br/>
but those who work without expecting reward<br/>
who attain the goal of meditation,<br/>
Theirs is true renunciation(<a href="/wiki/Sannyasa" title="Sannyasa">sanyāsā</a>).
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<i>Bhagavad Gita</i> 6.1<br/>Eknath Easwaran<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007139_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007139-148">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[note 14]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<h4><span id="Chapter_6:_Dhyanayoga_.28Aatmasamyamyoga.29_.2847_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_6:_Dhyanayoga_(Aatmasamyamyoga)_(47_verses)">Chapter 6: Dhyanayoga (Aatmasamyamyoga) (47 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Chapter 6: Dhyanayoga (Aatmasamyamyoga) (47 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the sixth chapter as <i>Dhyana yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Self-Restraint</i>, <i>The Practice of Meditation</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Meditation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter opens as a continuation of Krishna's teachings about selfless work and the personality of someone who has renounced the fruits that are found in chapter 5. Krishna says that such self-realized people are impartial to friends and enemies, are beyond good and evil, equally disposed to those who support them or oppose them because they have reached the summit of consciousness. The verses 6.10 and after proceed to summarize the principles of Yoga and meditation in the format similar to but simpler than Patanjali's <i>Yogasutra</i>. It discusses who is a true yogi, and what it takes to reach the state where one harbors no malice towards anyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007133–146_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007133–146-155">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012106–120_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012106–120-156">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_7:_Gyana_Vigyanayoga_.2830_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_7:_Gyana_Vigyanayoga_(30_verses)">Chapter 7: Gyana Vigyanayoga (30 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chapter 7: Gyana Vigyanayoga (30 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title this chapter as <i>Jnana–Vijnana yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Discernment</i>, <i>Wisdom from Realization</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Knowledge and Judgment</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter 7 once again opens with Krishna continuing his discourse. He discusses <i>jnana</i> (knowledge) and <i>vijnana</i> (realization, understanding) using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prakriti" title="Prakriti">Prakriti</a>-<a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">Purusha</a> (matter-Self) framework of the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> school of Hindu philosophy, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_(illusion)" title="Maya (illusion)">Maya</a>-<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> framework of its <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> school. The chapter states that evil is the consequence of ignorance and the attachment to the impermanent, delusive Maya. It equates self-knowledge and the union with Purusha (Krishna) as the Self to be the highest goal of any spiritual pursuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007147–156_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007147–156-157">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_8:_Akshara_Brahmayoga_.2828_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_8:_Akshara_Brahmayoga_(28_verses)">Chapter 8: Akshara Brahmayoga (28 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Chapter 8: Akshara Brahmayoga (28 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Aksara–Brahma yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Devotion to the One Supreme God</i>, <i>The Eternal Godhead</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Imperishable Brahman</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter opens with Arjuna asking questions such as what is Brahman and what is the nature of <i>karma</i>. Krishna states that his own highest nature is the imperishable Brahman, and that he lives in every creature as the <i>adhyatman</i>. Every being has an impermanent body and an eternal Self, and that "Krishna as Lord" lives within every creature. The chapter discusses cosmology, the nature of death and rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007157–168_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007157–168-158">[140]</a></sup> This chapter contains <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a> of the Bhagavad Gita. Importance of the last thought before death, differences between material and spiritual worlds, and light and dark paths that a Self takes after death are described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007157–168_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007157–168-158">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_9:_Raja_Vidya_Raja_Guhyayoga_.2834_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_9:_Raja_Vidya_Raja_Guhyayoga_(34_verses)">Chapter 9: Raja Vidya Raja Guhyayoga (34 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Chapter 9: Raja Vidya Raja Guhyayoga (34 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the ninth chapter as <i>Raja–Vidya–Raja–Guhya yoga</i>, <i>Religion by the Kingly Knowledge and the Kingly Mystery</i>, <i>The Royal Path</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Sovereign Science and Sovereign Secret</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-10"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> Chapter 9 opens with Krishna continuing his discourse as Arjuna listens. Krishna states that he is everywhere and in everything in an unmanifested form, yet he is not in any way limited by them. Eons end, everything dissolves and then he recreates another eon subjecting them to the laws of <i>Prakriti</i> (nature).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007169–178_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007169–178-159">[141]</a></sup> He equates himself to being the father and the mother of the universe, to being the <a href="/wiki/Om" title="Om">Om</a>, to the three Vedas, to the seed, the goal of life, the refuge and abode of all. The chapter recommends devotional worship of Krishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007169–178_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007169–178-159">[141]</a></sup> According to theologian Christopher Southgate, verses of this chapter of the <i>Gita</i> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">panentheistic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouthgate2005246_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouthgate2005246-160">[142]</a></sup> while German physicist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Max_Bernhard_Weinstein" title="Max Bernhard Weinstein">Max Bernhard Weinstein</a> deems the work <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">pandeistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinstein-India_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein-India-161">[143]</a></sup> It may, in fact, be neither of them, and its contents may have no definition with previously developed Western terms.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple,_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="452" decoding="async" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg/220px-Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg/330px-Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg/440px-Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple%2C_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhagavad_Gita_narrative_sculpture_on_a_column_in_the_Virupaksha_Temple,_Pattadakal_1885_photo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A frieze in the early 8th-century <a href="/wiki/Pattadakal" title="Pattadakal">Virupaksha temple (Pattadakal)</a> depicting <i>Mahabharata</i> scenes involving Arjuna-Krishna chariot. Pattadakal is a UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span id="Chapter_10:_Vibhutiyoga_.2842_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_10:_Vibhutiyoga_(42_verses)">Chapter 10: Vibhutiyoga (42 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Chapter 10: Vibhutiyoga (42 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Vibhuti–Vistara–yoga</i>, <i>Religion by the Heavenly Perfections</i>, <i>Divine Splendor</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Divine Manifestations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-11"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> When Arjuna asks the opulences (Vibhuti) of Krishna, he explains how all the entities are his forms. He reveals how his divine being in greater detail, as the ultimate cause of all material and spiritual existence, one who transcends all opposites and who is beyond any duality. Nevertheless, at Arjuna's behest, Krishna says the following are his major opulences. He says he is the <i>atman</i> in all beings, Arjuna's innermost Self, also compassionate Vishnu, <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a>, Indra, Shiva-Rudra, Ananta, Yama, as well as the Om, Vedic sages, time, Gayatri mantra, and the science of Self-knowledge. Krishna says, "Among the Pandavas, I am Arjuna," implying he is manifest in all the beings, including Arjuna. He also says that he is <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a> when he says, "Among the wielders of weapons, I am Rama". Arjuna accepts Krishna as the <i>purushottama</i> (Supreme Being).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007179–190_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007179–190-162">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_11:_Vishvarupa_Darshanayoga_.2855_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_11:_Vishvarupa_Darshanayoga_(55_verses)">Chapter 11: <a href="/wiki/Vishvarupa" title="Vishvarupa">Vishvarupa</a> Darshanayoga (55 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Chapter 11: Vishvarupa Darshanayoga (55 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vishvarupa" title="Vishvarupa">Vishvarupa</a></div>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Vishvarupa–Darshana yoga</i>, <i>The Manifesting of the One and Manifold</i>, <i>The Cosmic Vision</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Vision of the Cosmic Form</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-12"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> On Arjuna's request, Krishna displays his "universal form" (<i>Viśvarūpa</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007191–202_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007191–202-163">[145]</a></sup> This is an idea found in the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i> and many later Hindu texts, where it is a symbolism for <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">atman</a></i> (Self) and <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i> (Absolute Reality) eternally pervading all beings and all existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[147]</a></sup> Chapter 11, states Eknath Eswaran, describes Arjuna entering first into <i>savikalpa samadhi</i> (a particular), and then <i>nirvikalpa samadhi</i> (a universal) as he gets an understanding of Krishna. A part of the verse from this chapter was recited by Robert <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">Oppenheimer</a> as he witnessed the first atomic bomb explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007191–202_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007191–202-163">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_12:_Bhaktiyoga_.2820_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_12:_Bhaktiyoga_(20_verses)">Chapter 12: Bhaktiyoga (20 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chapter 12: Bhaktiyoga (20 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Bhakti yoga</i>, <i>The Religion of Faith</i>, <i>The Way of Love</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Devotion</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-13"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> In this chapter, Krishna glorifies the path of love and devotion to God. Krishna describes the process of devotional service (<a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">Bhakti yoga</a>). This chapter of the <i>Gita</i>, states Easwaran, offers a "vastly easier" path to most human beings to identify and love God in an anthropomorphic representation, in any form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007203–210_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007203–210-166">[148]</a></sup> He can be projected as "a merciful father, a divine mother, a wise friend, a passionate beloved, or even a mischievous child", according to Easwaran. The text states that combining "action with inner renunciation" with the love of Krishna as a personal God leads to peace. In the last eight verses of this chapter, Krishna states that he loves those who have compassion for all living beings, are content with whatever comes their way, who live a detached life that is impartial and selfless, unaffected by fleeting pleasure or pain, neither craving for praise nor depressed by criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007203–210_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007203–210-166">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_13:_Kshetra_Kshetragya_Vibhagayoga_.2834_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_13:_Kshetra_Kshetragya_Vibhagayoga_(34_verses)">Chapter 13: Kshetra Kshetragya Vibhagayoga (34 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Chapter 13: Kshetra Kshetragya Vibhagayoga (34 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript,_Sanskrit,_Malayalam_script,_Kerala.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="2444" decoding="async" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg/220px-16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg/330px-16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg/440px-16th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_palm_leaf_manuscript%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Malayalam_script%2C_Kerala.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Sanskrit, Malayalam script (Kerala)</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya,_Sanskrit,_Kannada_script,_Karnataka.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1764" data-file-width="1044" decoding="async" height="372" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg/220px-18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg/330px-18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg/440px-18th_century_Bhagavad_Gita_and_bhasya%2C_Sanskrit%2C_Kannada_script%2C_Karnataka.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Sanskrit, Kannada script (Karnataka)</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Bhagavad Gita</i> and related commentary literature exists in numerous Indian languages.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Some translators title this chapter as <i>Ksetra–Ksetrajna Vibhaga yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Separation of Matter and Spirit</i>, <i>The Field and the Knower</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Difference between the Field and Field-Knower</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-14"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter opens with Krishna continuing his discourse from the previous chapter. He describes the difference between transient perishable physical body (<i>kshetra</i>) and the immutable eternal Self (<i>kshetrajna</i>). The presentation explains the difference between <i>ahamkara</i> (ego) and <i>atman</i> (Self), from there between individual consciousness and universal consciousness. The knowledge of one's true self is linked to the realization of the Self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007211–220_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007211–220-168">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012216–221_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012216–221-169">[151]</a></sup> The 13th chapter of the <i>Gita</i> offers the clearest enunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> philosophy, states Basham, by explaining the difference between field (material world) and the knower (Self), <i>prakriti</i> and <i>purusha</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[152]</a></sup> According to Miller, this is the chapter which "redefines the battlefield as the human body, the material realm in which one struggles to know oneself" where human dilemmas are presented as a "symbolic field of interior warfare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller198612_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller198612-171">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_14:_Gunatraya_Vibhagayoga_.2827_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_14:_Gunatraya_Vibhagayoga_(27_verses)">Chapter 14: Gunatraya Vibhagayoga (27 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chapter 14: Gunatraya Vibhagayoga (27 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the fourteenth chapter as <i>Gunatraya–Vibhaga yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Separation from the Qualities</i>, <i>The Forces of Evolution</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Division of Three Gunas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-15"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The chapter once again opens with Krishna continuing his discourse from the previous chapter. Krishna explains the difference between <i>purusha</i> and <i>prakriti</i>, by mapping human experiences to three <i><a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">Guṇas</a></i> (tendencies, qualities).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007221–228_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007221–228-172">[154]</a></sup> These are listed as <i>sattva</i>, <i>rajas</i> and <i>tamas</i>. All phenomena and individual personalities are a combination of all three <i>gunas</i> in varying and ever-changing proportions. The <i>gunas</i> affect the ego, but not the Self, according to the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007221–228_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007221–228-172">[154]</a></sup> This chapter also relies on the Samkhya theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller198612–13,_59_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller198612–13,_59-173">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen2013=35–36_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen2013=35–36-174">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra201817–18_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra201817–18-175">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_15:_Purushottamayoga_.2820_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_15:_Purushottamayoga_(20_verses)">Chapter 15: Purushottamayoga (20 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Chapter 15: Purushottamayoga (20 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Purushottama yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Attaining the Supreme Krishna</i>, <i>The Supreme Self</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Supreme Purusha</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-16"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> The fifteenth chapter expounds on Krishna theology, in the Vaishnava Bhakti tradition of Hinduism. Krishna discusses the nature of God, according to Easwaran, wherein Krishna not only transcends impermanent body (matter), he also transcends the <i>atman</i> (Self) in every being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007229–234_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007229–234-176">[158]</a></sup> According to Franklin Edgerton, the verses in this chapter in association with select verses in other chapters make the metaphysics of the <i>Gita</i> to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dvaita" title="Dvaita">dualistic</a>. Its overall thesis is, states Edgerton, more complex however, because other verses teach the Upanishadic doctrines and "through its God the <i>Gita</i> seems after all to arrive at an ultimate monism; the essential part, the fundamental element, in every thing, is after all One — is God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Edgerton_1952_44–45_with_footnotes,_context:_pp._30–54_Part_2_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Edgerton_1952_44–45_with_footnotes,_context:_pp._30–54_Part_2-177">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_16:_Daivasura_Sampad_Vibhagayoga_.2824_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_16:_Daivasura_Sampad_Vibhagayoga_(24_verses)">Chapter 16: Daivasura Sampad Vibhagayoga (24 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Chapter 16: Daivasura Sampad Vibhagayoga (24 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Daivasura–Sampad–Vibhaga yoga</i>, <i>The Separateness of the Divine and Undivine</i>, <i>Two Paths</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Division between the Divine and the Demonic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-17"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-15"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-15"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> According to Easwaran, this is an unusual chapter where two types of human nature are expounded, one leading to happiness and the other to suffering. Krishna identifies these human traits to be divine and demonic respectively. He states that truthfulness, self-restraint, sincerity, love for others, desire to serve others, being detached, avoiding anger, avoiding harm to all living creatures, fairness, compassion and patience are marks of the divine nature. The opposite of these are demonic, such as cruelty, conceit, hypocrisy and being inhumane, states Krishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007235–242_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007235–242-178">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012253–257_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012253–257-180">[162]</a></sup> Some of the verses in Chapter 16 may be polemics directed against competing Indian religions, according to Basham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p95_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p95-56">[47]</a></sup> The competing tradition may be the materialists (<a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Charvaka</a>), states Fowler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012253–257_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012253–257-180">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_17:_Shraddhatraya_Vibhagayoga_.2828_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_17:_Shraddhatraya_Vibhagayoga_(28_verses)">Chapter 17: Shraddhatraya Vibhagayoga (28 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Chapter 17: Shraddhatraya Vibhagayoga (28 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Shraddhatraya-Vibhaga yoga</i>, <i>Religion by the Threefold Kinds of Faith</i>, <i>The Power of Faith</i>, or <i>The Yoga of the Threefold Faith</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-18"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-16"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-16"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> Krishna qualifies the three divisions of faith, thoughts, deeds, and even eating habits corresponding to the three modes (gunas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007243–250_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007243–250-181">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Chapter_18:_Moksha_Sanyasayoga_.2878_verses.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chapter_18:_Moksha_Sanyasayoga_(78_verses)">Chapter 18: Moksha Sanyasayoga (78 verses)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Chapter 18: Moksha Sanyasayoga (78 verses)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some translators title the chapter as <i>Moksha–Sanyasa yoga</i>, <i>Religion by Deliverance and Renunciation</i>, <i>Freedom and Renunciation</i>, or <i>The Yoga of Liberation and Renunciation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBG_22-19"><a href="#cite_note-EBG-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6_131-17"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran20075–6-131">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii_132-17"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra2018vii–viii-132">[116]</a></sup> In the final and long chapter, the <i>Gita</i> offers a final summary of its teachings in the previous chapters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[164]</a></sup> It covers many topics, states Easwaran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007251–265_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007251–265-183">[165]</a></sup> It begins with discussion of spiritual pursuits through <i>sannyasa</i> (renunciation, monastic life) and spiritual pursuits while living in the world as a householder. It re-emphasizes the <i>karma-phala-tyaga</i> teaching, or "act while renouncing the fruits of your action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007251–265_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007251–265-183">[165]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">Themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" This is an idea found in the <i>Rigveda</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[166]</a></sup> The <i>Vishvarupa</i> omniform has been interpreted as symbolism for Absolute Reality, God or Self that is in all creatures, everywhere, eternally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[168]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>

<div class="side-box-flex">

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nature_of_God">The nature of God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: The nature of God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> adopts the Upanishadic concept of Absolute Reality (<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>), a shift from the earlier ritual-driven Vedic religion to one abstracting and internalizing spiritual experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv-187">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41-188">[170]</a></sup> According to Jeaneane Fowler, the <i>Gita</i> builds on the Upanishadic Brahman theme, conceptualized to be that which is everywhere, unaffected, constant Absolute, indescribable and <i>nirguna</i> (abstract, without features). This Absolute in <i>Gita</i> is neither a He nor a She, but a "neuter principle", an "It or That".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv-187">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41-188">[170]</a></sup> Like some of the Upanishads, the <i>Gita</i> does not limit itself to the <i>nirguna</i> Brahman. It teaches both the abstract and the personalized Brahman (God), the latter in the form of Krishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv-187">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41_188-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner196930–32,_36–41-188">[170]</a></sup> It accomplishes this synthesis by projecting the <i>nirguna</i> Brahman as higher than <i>saguna</i> or personalized Brahman, where the <i>nirguna</i> Brahman "exists when everything else does not", states Fowler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxv_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxv-189">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryant2007p312_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryant2007p312-190">[172]</a></sup> The text blurs any distinction between the personalized God and impersonal Absolute Reality by amalgamating their equivalence, using it interchangeably in the later chapters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxv_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxv-189">[171]</a></sup> This theme has led scholars to call the <i>Gita</i> as panentheistic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxiii–xxiv-187">[169]</a></sup> theistic and monistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgerton195244–45_with_footnote_1_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgerton195244–45_with_footnote_1-191">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nature_of_Self">The nature of Self</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: The nature of Self">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i>, states Fowler, "thoroughly accepts" <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">atman</a></i> as a foundational concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxvi_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxvi-192">[174]</a></sup> In the Upanishads, this is the Brahmanical idea that all beings have a "permanent real self", the true essence, the Self it refers to as <i>Atman</i> (Self).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[note 15]</a></sup> In the Upanishads that preceded the <i>Gita</i> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad">Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a></i>, the salvific goal is to know and realize this Self, a knowledge that is devoid of the delusions of instinctive "I, mine, egoistic" typically connected with the body, material life processes that are impermanent and transient. The <i>Gita</i> accepts <i>atman</i> as the pure, unchanging, ultimate real essence, experiencer of one's being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxv,_xxxvii–xix_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxv,_xxxvii–xix-199">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nature_of_the_world">The nature of the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: The nature of the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> considers the world to be transient, all bodies and matter as impermanent. Everything that constitutes <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prakriti" title="Prakriti">prakriti</a></i> (nature, matter) is process driven and has a finite existence. It is born, grows, matures, decays and dies. It considers this transient reality as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_(illusion)" title="Maya (illusion)">Maya</a>. Like the Upanishads, the <i>Gita</i> focuses on what it considers as real in this world of change, impermanence, and finitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl-200">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner196913–15,_254_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner196913–15,_254-201">[182]</a></sup> To build its theological framework about the world, the text relies on the theories found in Samkhya and Vedanta schools of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner196913–15,_254_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner196913–15,_254-201">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brahman-atman">Brahman-atman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Brahman-atman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Upanishads developed the equation "Atman = Brahman", states Fowler, and this belief is central to the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl-200">[181]</a></sup> This equation is, however, interpreted in a number of ways by different sub-schools of Vedanta. In the <i>Gita</i>, the Self of each human being is considered to be identical to every other human being and all beings, but it "does not support an identity with the Brahman", according to Fowler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxxix–xl-200">[181]</a></sup> According to Raju, the <i>Gita</i> supports this identity and spiritual monism, but as a form of synthesis with a personal God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup> According to Edgerton, the author(s) of the <i>Gita</i> rely on their concept of personalized God (Krishna) to ultimately arrive at an ultimate monism, where the devotee ultimately realizes that Krishna is the essential part, the Real, the fundamental element in him, everyone and everything. Krishna is all and One.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Edgerton_1952_44–45_with_footnotes,_context:_pp._30–54_Part_2_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Edgerton_1952_44–45_with_footnotes,_context:_pp._30–54_Part_2-177">[159]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Huston_Smith" title="Huston Smith">Huston Smith</a>, the <i>Gita</i> is teaching that "when one sees the entire universe as pervaded by the single Universal Spirit [Krishna], one contemplates, marvels, and falls in love with its amazing glory. [...] Having experienced that Truth oneself, all doubts are dispelled. This is how the flower of devotion evolves into the fruit of knowledge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xvii_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xvii-202">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Means_to_God">Means to God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Means to God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> teaches several spiritual paths – jnana, bhakti and karma – to the divine. However, states Fowler, it "does not raise any of these to a status that excludes the others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl-203">[184]</a></sup> The theme that unites these paths in the <i>Gita</i> is "inner renunciation" where one is unattached to personal rewards during one's spiritual journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl-203">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Karma_yoga">Karma yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Karma yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">Karma yoga</a></div>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> teaches the path of Karma yoga in Chapter 3 and others. It upholds the necessity of action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–iv_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–iv-204">[185]</a></sup> However, this action should "not simply follow spiritual injunctions", without any attachment to personal rewards or because of craving for fruits. The <i>Gita</i> teaches, according to Fowler, that the action should be undertaken after proper knowledge has been applied to gain the full perspective of "what the action should be".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–xliv_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–xliv-205">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of such detached action is also called <i><a href="/wiki/Nishkama_Karma" title="Nishkama Karma">Nishkama Karma</a></i>, a term not used in the <i>Gita</i> but equivalent to other terms such as <i>karma-phala-tyaga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–xliv_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xliii–xliv-205">[186]</a></sup> This is where one determines what the right action ought to be and then acts while being detached to personal outcomes, to fruits, to success or failure. A karma yogi finds such work inherently fulfilling and satisfying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhawuk2011p147_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhawuk2011p147-207">[188]</a></sup> To a <i>karma yogi</i>, right work done well is a form of prayer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[189]</a></sup> and <i>karma yoga</i> is the path of selfless action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coward2012p142_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coward2012p142-209">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Mahatma Gandhi (Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi), the object of the <i>Gita</i> is to show the way to attain self-realization, and this "can be achieved by selfless action, by desireless action; by renouncing fruits of action; by dedicating all activities to God, i.e., by surrendering oneself to Him body and Self." Gandhi called the <i>Gita</i> "The Gospel of Selfless Action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gandhi_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-gandhi-210">[191]</a></sup> According to Jonardon Ganeri, the premise of "disinterested action" is one of the important ethical concepts in the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhakti_yoga">Bhakti yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Bhakti yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">Bhakti yoga</a></div>
