<div class="description">Verse 2.56, "who is a sage" (14 secs)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>In the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, bhakti is characterized as the "loving devotion, a longing, surrender, trust and adoration" of the divine Krishna as the <i>ishta-devata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xlii–xliii_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xlii–xliii-212">[193]</a></sup> While bhakti is mentioned in many chapters, the idea gathers momentum after verse 6.30, and it is chapter 12 where the idea is sustainly developed. According to Fowler, the bhakti in the <i>Gita</i> does not imply renunciation of "action", but the bhakti effort is assisted with "right knowledge" and dedication to one's <i>dharma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xlii–xliii_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xlii–xliii-212">[193]</a></sup> Theologian <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Cornille" title="Catherine Cornille">Catherine Cornille</a> writes, "The text [of the <i>Gita</i>] offers a survey of the different possible disciplines for attaining liberation through knowledge (<i>Jnana</i>), action (karma), and loving devotion to God (bhakti), focusing on the latter as both the easiest and the highest path to salvation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to M. R. Sampatkumaran, a <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> scholar, the <i>Gita</i> message is that mere knowledge of the scriptures cannot lead to final release, but "devotion, meditation, and worship are essential."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[195]</a></sup> The <i>Gita</i> likely spawned a "powerful devotionalism" movement, states Fowler, because the text and this path was simpler, available to everyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxii–xxiv,_xlii–xliii_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xxii–xxiv,_xlii–xliii-215">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jnana_yoga">Jnana yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Jnana yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">Jnana yoga</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7262" data-file-width="5146" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/220px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/330px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg/440px-Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma_-_Sankaracharya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> with Disciples</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ravi_Varma" title="Raja Ravi Varma">Raja Ravi Varma</a> (1904); Shankara published 700 verses of the <i>Gita</i> (800 CE), now the standard version.</div></div></div>
<p>Jnana yoga is the path of knowledge, wisdom, and direct realization of the Brahman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl–xlii,_89–93_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xl–xlii,_89–93-216">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[198]</a></sup> In the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, it is also referred to as <i>buddhi yoga</i> and its goal is self-realization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016266_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016266-218">[199]</a></sup> The text states that this is the path that intellectuals tend to prefer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[200]</a></sup> The chapter 4 of the Bhagavad Gita is dedicated to the general exposition of <i>jnana yoga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler201272–90_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler201272–90-220">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009201–242_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009201–242-221">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Gita</i> praises the path, calling the jnana yogin to be exceedingly dear to Krishna, but adds that the path is steep and difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xli–xlii_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012xli–xlii-222">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Synthesis_of_yogas.2C_Raja_yoga"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis_of_yogas,_Raja_yoga">Synthesis of yogas, Raja yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Synthesis of yogas, Raja yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sivananda's commentary regards the eighteen chapters of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> as having a progressive order, by which Krishna leads "Arjuna up the ladder of Yoga from one rung to another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[204]</a></sup> The influential commentator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madhusudana_Sarasvati" title="Madhusudana Sarasvati">Madhusudana Sarasvati</a> divided the <i>Gita</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s eighteen chapters into three sections of six chapters each. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swami_Gambhirananda" title="Swami Gambhirananda">Swami Gambhirananda</a> characterises Madhusudana Sarasvati's system as a successive approach in which Karma yoga leads to Bhakti yoga, which in turn leads to Jnana yoga:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Chapters 1–6 = Karma yoga, the means to the final goal</li>
<li>Chapters 7–12 = Bhakti yoga or devotion</li>
<li>Chapters 13–18 = Jnana yoga or knowledge, the goal itself</li></ul>
<p>Some scholars treat the "yoga of meditation" to be a distinct fourth path taught in the <i>Gita</i>, referring to it as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_yoga" title="Raja yoga">Raja yoga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xii-96">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson200692–93,_133–134_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson200692–93,_133–134-97">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1986131,_194–196_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1986131,_194–196-225">[206]</a></sup> Others consider it as a progressive stage or a combination of Karma yoga and Bhakti yoga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1986123_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1986123-226">[207]</a></sup> Some, such as Adi Shankara, have considered its discussion in the 13th chapter of the <i>Gita</i> and elsewhere to be an integral part of Jnana yoga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2009p179_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2009p179-228">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Asceticism.2C_renunciation_and_ritualism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Asceticism,_renunciation_and_ritualism">Asceticism, renunciation and ritualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Asceticism, renunciation and ritualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> rejects ascetic life, renunciation as well as Brahminical Vedic ritualism where outwardly actions or non-action are considered a means of personal rewards in this life, after-life or a means of liberation. It instead recommends the pursuit of an active life where the individual adopts "inner renunciation", acts to fulfill what he determines to be his <i>dharma</i>, without craving for or concerns about personal rewards, viewing this as an "inner sacrifice to the personal God for a higher good".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson2004xi_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2004xi-229">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Edwin Bryant, the Indologist with publications on Krishna-related Hindu traditions, the <i>Gita</i> rejects "actionless behavior" found in some Indic monastic traditions. It also "relegates the sacrificial system of the early Vedic literature to a path that goes nowhere because it is based on desires", states Bryant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dharma">Dharma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Dharma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a></div>
<p>Dharma is a prominent paradigm of the <i>Mahabharata</i>, and it is referenced in the <i>Gita</i> as well. The term <i>dharma</i> has a number of meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> Fundamentally, it means "what is right".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> Contextually, it also means the essence of "duty, law, class, social norms, ritual and cosmos itself" in the text, in the sense "the way things should be in all these different dimensions", states Fowler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> According to Zaehner, the term <i>dharma</i> means "duty" in <i>Gita</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s context, in verse 2.7 refers to the "right [and wrong]", and in 14.27 to "eternal law of righteousness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner1969123,_382_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner1969123,_382-233">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Few verses in the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> deal with dharma, according to the Indologist Paul Hacker, but the theme of dharma is important in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995260_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995260-234">[215]</a></sup> In Chapter 1, responding to <a href="/wiki/Arjuna" title="Arjuna">Arjuna</a>'s despondency, <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> asks him to follow his <i>sva-dharma</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker19581–15_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker19581–15-235">[216]</a></sup> "the dharma that belongs to a particular man (Arjuna) as a member of a particular <i><a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">varna</a></i>, (i.e., the <i>kshatriya</i> – the warrior varna)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker1958261_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker1958261-236">[217]</a></sup> According to Paul Hacker, the term <i>dharma</i> has additional meanings in the context of Arjuna. It is more broadly, the "duty" and a "metaphysically congealed act" for Arjuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261-237">[218]</a></sup> According to the Indologist Jacqueline Hirst, the <i>dharma</i> theme is "of significance only at the beginning and end of the <i>Gita</i>" and this may have been a way to perhaps link the <i>Gita</i> to the context of the <i>Mahabharata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirst1997p48_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirst1997p48-238">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Malinar, "Arjuna's crisis and some of the arguments put forward to call him to action are connected to the debates on war and peace in the <i><a href="/wiki/Udyoga_Parva" title="Udyoga Parva">Udyoga Parva</a></i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736-239">[220]</a></sup> The <i>Udyoga Parva</i> presents many views about the nature of a warrior, his duty and what calls for heroic action. While <a href="/wiki/Duryodhana" title="Duryodhana">Duryodhana</a> presents it as a matter of status, social norms, and fate, <a href="/wiki/Vidura" title="Vidura">Vidura</a> states that the heroic warrior never submits, knows no fear and has the duty to protect people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736–41_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736–41-240">[221]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Bhishma_Parva" title="Bhishma Parva">Bhishma Parva</a></i> sets the stage of two ideologies in conflict and two massive armies gathered for what each considers as a righteous and necessary war. In this context, the <i>Gita</i> advises Arjuna to do his holy duty (<i>sva-dharma</i>) as a warrior, fight and kill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller20043_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller20043-241">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200459_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200459-242">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736–39_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalinar200736–39-243">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Indologist Barbara Miller, the text frames heroism not in terms of physical abilities, but instead in terms of effort and inner commitment to fulfill a warrior's <i>dharma</i> in the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8-244">[225]</a></sup> War is depicted as a horror, the impending slaughter a cause of self-doubts, yet at stake is the spiritual struggle against evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8-244">[225]</a></sup> The <i>Gita</i> message emphasizes that the personal moral confusion and struggle must be addressed, the warrior needs to rise beyond "personal and social values" and understand what is at stake and "why he must fight", states Miller. The text explores the "paradoxical interconnectedness of disciplined action and freedom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8_244-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller19861–8-244">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Field_of_Dharma"><i>The Field of Dharma</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: The Field of Dharma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The first reference to dharma in the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> occurs in its first verse, where <a href="/wiki/Dhritarashtra" title="Dhritarashtra">Dhritarashtra</a> refers to the Kurukshetra, the location of the battlefield, as the <i>Field of Dharma</i>, "The Field of Righteousness or Truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> According to Fowler, dharma in this verse may refer to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanatana_dharma" title="Sanatana dharma">sanatana dharma</a></i>, "what Hindus understand as their religion, for it is a term that encompasses wide aspects of religious and traditional thought and is more readily used for religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> Therefore, 'Field of action' implies the field of righteousness, where truth will eventually triumph, states Fowler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> According to Jacqueline Hirst, the "field of <i>dharma</i>" phrase in the <i>Gita</i> epitomizes that the struggle concerns <i>dharma</i> itself. This <i>dharma</i> has "resonances at many different levels".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>"The Field of Dharma" is also called the "Field of action" by <a href="/wiki/Sri_Aurobindo" title="Sri Aurobindo">Sri Aurobindo</a>, a freedom fighter and philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler20122_232-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler20122-232">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan</a>, a professor of philosophy at the Oxford University and the second president of India, saw "The Field of Dharma" as the world (Bhavsagar), which is a "battleground for moral struggle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegory_of_war">Allegory of war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Allegory of war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Unlike any other religious scripture, the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> broadcasts its message in the centre of the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[228]</a></sup> Several modern Indian writers have interpreted the battlefield setting as an allegory of "the war within".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200715_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran200715-248">[229]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eknath_Easwaran" title="Eknath Easwaran">Eknath Easwaran</a> writes that the <i>Gita</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s subject is "the war within, the struggle for self-mastery that every human being must wage if he or she is to emerge from life victorious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Swami_Nikhilananda" title="Swami Nikhilananda">Swami Nikhilananda</a>, takes Arjuna as an allegory of Ātman, Krishna as an allegory of <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, Arjuna's chariot as the body, and Dhritarashtra as the ignorance filled mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[note 16]</a></sup> Nikhilananda's allegorical interpretation is shared by Huston Smith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009x-65">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a> interprets the first discourse in the <i>Gita</i> as well as the "Kurushetra war" allegorically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[231]</a></sup> Vivekananda states, "when we sum up its esoteric significance, it means the war which is constantly going on within man between the tendencies of good and evil".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SV-vol4_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-SV-vol4-252">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a>, in his commentary on the <i>Gita</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[233]</a></sup> interprets the battle as "an allegory in which the battlefield is the soul and Arjuna, man's higher impulses struggling against evil".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurobindo" title="Aurobindo">Aurobindo</a>'s view, Krishna was a historical figure, but his significance in the <i>Gita</i> is as a "symbol of the divine dealings with humanity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[235]</a></sup> while Arjuna typifies a "struggling human soul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THD_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-THD-256">[236]</a></sup> However, Aurobindo rejected the interpretation that the <i>Gita</i>, and the <i>Mahabharata</i> by extension, is only "an allegory of the inner life", and it has nothing to do with our outward human life and actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THD_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-THD-256">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[note 17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion_of_just_war_and_duty">Promotion of just war and duty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Promotion of just war and duty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Other scholars such as Steven Rosen, Laurie L. Patton and Stephen Mitchell have seen in the <i>Gita</i> a religious defense of the warrior class's (<a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kshatriya</a> <a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">Varna</a>) duty (<i>svadharma</i>), which is to conduct combat and war with courage and do not see this as only an allegorical teaching, but also a real defense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Just_war" title="Just war">just war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence leaders</a> like <a href="/wiki/Lala_Lajpat_Rai" title="Lala Lajpat Rai">Lala Lajpat Rai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Bal Gangadhar Tilak</a> saw the <i>Gita</i> as a text which defended war when necessary and used it to promote armed rebellion against colonial rule. Lajpat Rai wrote an article on the "Message of the Bhagavad Gita". He saw the main message as the bravery and courage of Arjuna to fight as a warrior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-260">[239]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Bal Gangadhar Tilak</a> saw the Gita as defending killing when necessary for the betterment of society, such as, for example, the killing of <a href="/wiki/Afzal_Khan_(general)" title="Afzal Khan (general)">Afzal Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-260">[239]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moksha:_Liberation">Moksha: Liberation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Moksha: Liberation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a></div>
<p>Liberation or <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> philosophy is not something that can be acquired. <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Ātman</a></i> (Self) and Self-knowledge, along with the loss of egotistic ignorance, the goal of <i>moksha</i>, is something that is always present as the essence of the self, and must be realized by each person by one's own effort. While the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> largely uphold such a monistic viewpoint of liberation, the Bhagavad Gita also accommodates the dualistic and theistic aspects of <i>moksha</i>. The <i>Gita</i>, while including impersonal <i>Nirguna Brahman</i> as the goal, mainly revolves around the relationship between the Self and a personal God or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saguna_Brahman" title="Saguna Brahman">Saguna Brahman</a></i>. A synthesis of knowledge, devotion, and desireless action is offered by Krishna as a spectrum of choices to Arjuna; the same combination is suggested to the reader as a way to moksha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[240]</a></sup> Christopher Chapple – a Comparative Theology scholar focusing on Indian religions, in <a href="/wiki/Winthrop_Sargeant" title="Winthrop Sargeant">Winthrop Sargeant</a> translation of the <i>Gita</i>, states, "In the model presented by the Bhagavad Gītā, every aspect of life is in fact a way of salvation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxii–xxiii_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxii–xxiii-262">[241]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pancaratra_Agama">Pancaratra Agama</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Pancaratra Agama">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="486" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg/170px-Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg/255px-Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg/340px-Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vasudeva_Krishna_on_a_coin_of_Agathocles_of_Bactria_circa_180_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V%C4%81sudeva-Krishna" title="Vāsudeva-Krishna">Vāsudeva-Krishna</a>, on a coin of <a href="/wiki/Agathocles_of_Bactria" title="Agathocles of Bactria">Agathocles of Bactria</a>, c.<span class="nowrap"> </span>180<span class="nowrap"> </span>BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-US-263">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[243]</a></sup> This is "the earliest unambiguous image" of the deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRILL_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-BRILL-265">[244]</a></sup></center></div></div></div>
<p>According to Dennis Hudson, there is an overlap between Vedic and Tantric rituals with the teachings found in the Bhagavad Gita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson20029,_160–163_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson20029,_160–163-266">[245]</a></sup> He places the <i>Pancaratra Agama</i> in the last three or four centuries of 1st-millennium BCE, and proposes that both the tantric and vedic, the Agama and the Gita share the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V%C4%81sudeva-Krishna" title="Vāsudeva-Krishna">Vāsudeva-Krishna</a> roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson2002133_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson2002133-267">[246]</a></sup> Some of the ideas in the Bhagavad Gita connect it to the <i>Shatapatha Brahmana</i> of <i><a href="/wiki/Yajurveda" title="Yajurveda">Yajurveda</a></i>. The <i>Shatapatha Brahmana</i>, for example, mentions the absolute Purusha who dwells in every human being. A story in this vedic text, states Hudson, highlights the meaning of the name Vāsudeva as the 'shining one (deva) who dwells (vasu) in all things and in whom all things dwell', and the meaning of Vishnu to be the 'pervading actor'. In Bhagavad Gita, similarly, '<a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> identified himself both with <a href="/wiki/V%C4%81sudeva" title="Vāsudeva">Vāsudeva</a>, Vishnu and their meanings'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson2002156–157_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson2002156–157-268">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[note 18]</a></sup> The ideas at the center of Vedic rituals in <i>Shatapatha Brahmana</i> and the teachings of the Bhagavad Gita revolve around this absolute Person, the primordial genderless absolute, which is same as the goal of Pancaratra Agama and Tantra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson2002162–163_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson2002162–163-271">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first English translation of the Bhagavad Gita was published by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wilkins" title="Charles Wilkins">Charles Wilkins</a> in 1785.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gita-Wilkins_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gita-Wilkins-272">[250]</a></sup> The Wilkins translation had an introduction to the Gita by Warren Hastings. Soon the work was translated into other European languages such as French (1787), German, and Russian. In 1849, the Weleyan Mission Press, Bangalore published <i>The Bhagavat-Geeta, Or, Dialogues of Krishna and Arjoon in Eighteen Lectures</i>, with Sanskrit, <a href="/wiki/Kannada" title="Kannada">Canarese</a> and English in parallel columns, edited by Rev. John Garrett, and the efforts being supported by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Cubbon" title="Mark Cubbon">Sir. Mark Cubbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gita-Garrett_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gita-Garrett-273">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:505px;max-width:505px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2110" data-file-width="1254" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg/150px-1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg/225px-1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg/300px-1785_Bhagavad_Gita_translation_English.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:145px;max-width:145px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2162" data-file-width="1226" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg/143px-1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg/215px-1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg/286px-1787_Bhagavad_Gita_re-translation_French.jpg 2x" width="143"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhagvat_-_Geeta,_Wesleyan_Mission_Press,_Bangalore,_1849.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5578" data-file-width="4394" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg/200px-Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg/300px-Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg/400px-Bhagvat_-_Geeta%2C_Wesleyan_Mission_Press%2C_Bangalore%2C_1849.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Cover pages of early <i>Gita</i> translations. Left: Charles Wilkins (1785); Center: Parraud re-translation of Wilkins (1787); Right: Wesleyan Mission Press (1849).</div></div></div></div>
<p>In 1981, Larson stated that "a complete listing of <i>Gita</i> translations and a related secondary bibliography would be nearly endless".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson81_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson81-274">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 514">: 514 </span></sup> According to Larson, there is "a massive translational tradition in English, pioneered by the British, solidly grounded philologically by the French and Germans, provided with its indigenous roots by a rich heritage of modern Indian comment and reflection, extended into various disciplinary areas by Americans, and having generated in our time a broadly based cross-cultural awareness of the importance of the Bhagavad Gita both as an expression of a specifically Indian spirituality and as one of the great religious "classics" of all time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson81_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson81-274">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 518">: 518 </span></sup>
</p><p>According to Sargeant, the <i>Gita</i> is "said to have been translated at least 200 times, in both poetic and prose forms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20091_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20091-275">[253]</a></sup> Richard Davis cites a count by Callewaert &amp; Hemraj in 1982 of 1,891 translations of the Bhagavad Gita in 75 languages, including 273 in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis2014154–155_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis2014154–155-276">[254]</a></sup> These translations vary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[255]</a></sup> and are in part an interpretative reconstruction of the original Sanskrit text that differ in their "friendliness to the reader",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis2014159–161_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis2014159–161-278">[256]</a></sup> and in the amount of "violence to the original <i>Gita</i> text" that the translation does.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxv_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxv-279">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[note 19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The translations and interpretations of the <i>Gita</i> have been so diverse that these have been used to support apparently contradictory political and philosophical values. For example, Galvin Flood and Charles Martin note that interpretations of the <i>Gita</i> have been used to support "pacifism to aggressive nationalism" in politics, from "monism to theism" in philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[262]</a></sup> According to William Johnson, the synthesis of ideas in the <i>Gita</i> is such that it can bear almost any shade of interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[263]</a></sup> A translation "can never fully reproduce an original and no translation is transparent", states Richard Davis, but in the case of <i>Gita</i> the linguistic and cultural distance for many translators is large and steep which adds to the challenge and affects the translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201480–81_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201480–81-287">[264]</a></sup> For some native translators, their personal beliefs, motivations, and subjectivity affect their understanding, their choice of words and interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis2014157–158_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis2014157–158-288">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986xvi–xxvii_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986xvi–xxvii-290">[267]</a></sup> Some translations by Indians, with or without Western co-translators, have "orientalist", "apologetic", "Neo-Vedantin" or "guru phenomenon" bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson81_274-2"><a href="#cite_note-larson81-274">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 525–530">: 525–530 </span></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-External_links plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-external_links" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section's <b>use of <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:External_links" title="Wikipedia:External links">external links</a> may not follow Wikipedia's policies or guidelines</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by removing <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_mirror_or_a_repository_of_links,_images,_or_media_files" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">excessive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:External_links" title="Wikipedia:External links">inappropriate</a> external links, and converting useful links where appropriate into <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">footnote references</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
