<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> scholar Robert Minor, the <i>Gita</i> is "probably the most translated of any Asian text", but many modern versions heavily reflect the views of the organization or person who does the translating and distribution. In Minor's view, the Harvard scholar Franklin Edgerton's English translation and Richard Garbe's German translation are closer to the text than many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1982xx–xxi_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1982xx–xxi-305">[269]</a></sup> According to Larson, the Edgerton translation is remarkably faithful, but it is "harsh, stilted, and syntactically awkward" with an "orientalist" bias and lacks "appreciation of the text's contemporary religious significance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson81_274-4"><a href="#cite_note-larson81-274">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 524">: 524 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Gita_in_other_languages">The Gita in other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: The Gita in other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> has also been translated into European languages other than English. In the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries, in the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>, multiple discrete Persian translations of the <i>Gita</i> were completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[270]</a></sup> In 1808, passages from the <i>Gita</i> were part of the first direct translation of Sanskrit into German, appearing in a book through which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Schlegel" title="Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel">Friedrich Schlegel</a> became known as the founder of Indian philology in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[271]</a></sup> The most significant French translation of the <i>Gita</i>, according to J. A. B. van Buitenen, was published by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Senart" title="Émile Senart">Émile Senart</a> in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen2013xii;_Emile_Senart's_Translation:_Wikisource_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.A.B._van_Buitenen2013xii;_Emile_Senart's_Translation:_Wikisource-308">[272]</a></sup> More recently, a new French translation was produced by the indianist Alain Porte in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[273]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rambhadracharya" title="Rambhadracharya">Swami Rambhadracharya</a> released the first <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">Braille</a> version of the scripture, with the original Sanskrit text and a Hindi commentary, on 30 November 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[web 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gita_Press" title="Gita Press">Gita Press</a> has published the Gita in multiple Indian languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[274]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/R._Raghava_Iyengar" title="R. Raghava Iyengar">R. Raghava Iyengar</a> translated the <i>Gita</i> into Tamil in sandam metre poetic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[275]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhaktivedanta_Book_Trust" title="Bhaktivedanta Book Trust">Bhaktivedanta Book Trust</a> associated with ISKCON has re-translated and published A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada's 1972 English translation of the <i>Gita</i> in 56 non-Indian languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[note 33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vinoba_Bhave" title="Vinoba Bhave">Vinoba Bhave</a> has written the Geeta in <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi language</a> as Geetai i.e. Mother Geeta in the similar shloka form.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paramahansa_Yogananda" title="Paramahansa Yogananda">Paramahansa Yogananda</a>'s commentary on the Bhagavad Gita called <i><a href="/wiki/God_Talks_with_Arjuna:_The_Bhagavad_Gita" title="God Talks with Arjuna: The Bhagavad Gita">God Talks with Arjuna: The Bhagavad Gita</a></i> has been translated into Spanish, German, Thai and Hindi so far. The book is significant in that unlike other commentaries of the Bhagavad Gita, which focus on <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">karma yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">jnana yoga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">bhakti yoga</a> in relation to the Gita, Yogananda's work stresses the training of one's mind, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_yoga" title="Raja yoga">raja yoga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_&amp;_Francis_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_&amp;_Francis-318">[280]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Bhashya_.28commentaries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhashya_(commentaries)">Bhashya (commentaries)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Bhashya (commentaries)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Bhagavad Gita</i> integrates various schools of thought, notably Vedanta, Samkhya and Yoga, and other theistic ideas. It remains a popular text for commentators belonging to various philosophical schools. However, its composite nature also leads to varying interpretations of the text and historic scholars have written <i><a href="/wiki/Bhashya" title="Bhashya">bhashya</a></i> (commentaries) on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mysore_Hiriyanna" title="Mysore Hiriyanna">Mysore Hiriyanna</a>, the <i>Gita</i> is "one of the hardest books to interpret, which accounts for the numerous commentaries on it—each differing from the rest in one essential point or the other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[282]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Richard Davis, the <i>Gita</i> has attracted much scholarly interest in Indian history and some 227 commentaries have survived in the Sanskrit language alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis201455–56_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis201455–56-321">[283]</a></sup> It has also attracted commentaries in regional vernacular languages for centuries, such as the one by Sant (Saint) Dnyaneshwar in Marathi language (13th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dnyandevfull_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dnyandevfull-322">[284]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_commentaries">Classical commentaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Classical commentaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is referred to in the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutras" title="Brahma Sutras">Brahma Sutras</a></i>, and numerous scholars including <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Shankara</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_(philosopher)" title="Bhāskara (philosopher)">Bhaskara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abhinavagupta" title="Abhinavagupta">Abhinavagupta</a> of Shaivism tradition, <a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhvacharya</a> wrote commentaries on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200460_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200460-323">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996124_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996124-324">[286]</a></sup> Many of these commentators state that the <i>Gita</i> is "meant to be a <i>moksa-shastra</i> (<i>moksasatra</i>), and not a <i>dharmasastra</i>, an <i>arthasastra</i> or a <i>kamasastra</i>", states Sharma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".C5.9Aa.E1.B9.85kara_.28c._800_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Śaṅkara_(c._800_CE)">Śaṅkara (c. 800 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Śaṅkara (c. 800 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The oldest and most influential surviving commentary was published by <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> (Śaṅkarācārya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zaehner,_p._3_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zaehner,_p._3-326">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_1996_page=124_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_1996_page=124-327">[289]</a></sup> Shankara interprets the <i>Gita</i> in a monist, nondualistic tradition (<a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[290]</a></sup> Shankara prefaces his comments by stating that the <i>Gita</i> is popular among the laity, that the text has been studied and commented upon by earlier scholars (these texts have not survived), but "I have found that to the laity it appears to teach diverse and quite contradictory doctrines". He calls the <i>Gita</i> as "an epitome of the essentials of the whole <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedic teaching</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[291]</a></sup> To Shankara, the teaching of the <i>Gita</i> is to shift an individual's focus from the outer, impermanent, fleeting objects of desire and senses to the inner, permanent, eternal atman-Brahman-Vasudeva that is identical, in everything and in every being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Abhinavagupta_.28c._1000_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Abhinavagupta_(c._1000_CE)">Abhinavagupta (c. 1000 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Abhinavagupta (c. 1000 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Abhinavagupta was a theologian and philosopher of the Kashmir Shaivism (<a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>) tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_1996_page=124_327-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_1996_page=124-327">[289]</a></sup> He wrote a commentary on the <i>Gita</i> as <i>Gitartha-Samgraha</i>, which has survived into the modern era. The <i>Gita</i> text he commented on is a slightly different recension than the one of Adi Shankara. He interprets its teachings in the Shaiva Advaita (monism) tradition quite similar to Adi Shankara, but with the difference that he considers both Self and matter to be metaphysically real and eternal. Their respective interpretations of <i>jnana yoga</i> are also somewhat different, and Abhinavagupta uses Atman, Brahman, Shiva, and Krishna interchangeably. Abhinavagupta's commentary is notable for its citations of more ancient scholars, in a style similar to Adi Shankara. However, the texts he quotes have not survived into the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[293]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="R.C4.81m.C4.81nuja_.28c._1100_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rāmānuja_(c._1100_CE)">Rāmānuja (c. 1100 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Rāmānuja (c. 1100 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a> was a Hindu theologian, philosopher, and an exponent of the Sri Vaishnavism (<a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>) tradition in 11th and early 12th century. Like his Vedanta peers, Ramanuja wrote a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhasya" title="Bhasya">bhashya</a></i> (commentary) on the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[294]</a></sup> Ramanuja's disagreed with Adi Shankara's interpretation of the <i>Gita</i> as a text on nondualism (Self and Brahman are identical), and instead interpreted it as a form of dualistic and qualified monism philosophy (<a href="/wiki/Vishishtadvaita" title="Vishishtadvaita">Vishishtadvaita</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[296]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Madhva_.28c._1250_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Madhva_(c._1250_CE)">Madhva (c. 1250 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Madhva (c. 1250 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhva</a>, a commentator of the Dvaita (modern taxonomy) Tatvavada (actually quoted by Madhva) Vedanta school,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_1996_page=124_327-2"><a href="#cite_note-Flood_1996_page=124-327">[289]</a></sup> wrote a commentary on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, which exemplifies the thinking of the Tatvavada school (<a href="/wiki/Dvaita_Vedanta" title="Dvaita Vedanta">Dvaita Vedanta</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zaehner,_p._3_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zaehner,_p._3-326">[288]</a></sup> According to Christopher Chapelle, in the Madhva's school there is "an eternal and complete distinction between the Supreme, the many Selfs, and matter and its divisions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxiii–xxiv_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxiii–xxiv-335">[297]</a></sup> His commentary on the <i>Gita</i> is called <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Gita Bhāshya</i></span>. Madhva's commentary has attracted secondary works by pontiffs of the <a href="/wiki/Dvaita_Vedanta" title="Dvaita Vedanta">Dvaita Vedanta</a> monasteries such as <a href="/wiki/Padmanabha_Tirtha" title="Padmanabha Tirtha">Padmanabha Tirtha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jayatirtha" title="Jayatirtha">Jayatirtha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Raghavendra_Tirtha" title="Raghavendra Tirtha">Raghavendra Tirtha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[298]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ke.C5.9Bava_K.C4.81.C5.9Bm.C4.ABri_.28c._1479_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Keśava_Kāśmīri_(c._1479_CE)">Keśava Kāśmīri (c. 1479 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Keśava Kāśmīri (c. 1479 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Keśava Kāśmīri Bhaṭṭa, a commentator of Dvaitādvaita Vedanta school, wrote a commentary on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> named <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Tattva-prakāśikā</i></span>. The text states that <i>Dasasloki</i>—possibly authored by Nimbarka—teaches the essence of the <i>Gita</i>; the <i>Gita tattva prakashika</i> interprets the <i>Gita</i> also in a hybrid monist-dualist manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p37_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p37-337">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-338">[300]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Vallabha_.281481.E2.80.931533_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vallabha_(1481–1533_CE)">Vallabha (1481–1533 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Vallabha (1481–1533 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vallabhacharya" title="Vallabhacharya">Vallabha</a>, the proponent of "Suddhadvaita" or pure non-dualism, wrote a commentary on the <i>Gita</i>, the <i>Sattvadipika</i>. According to him, the true Self is the Supreme Brahman. <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> is the most important means of attaining liberation.
</p>
<h4><span id="Gau.E1.B8.8D.C4.ABya_Vai.E1.B9.A3.E1.B9.87ava_Commentaries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gauḍīya_Vaiṣṇava_Commentaries">Gauḍīya Vaiṣṇava Commentaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Gauḍīya Vaiṣṇava Commentaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chaitanya_Mahaprabhu" title="Chaitanya Mahaprabhu">Chaitanya Mahaprabhu</a> (b. 1486 CE). Commentaries on various parts of the <i>Gita</i> are in the <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavism</a> Bhakti Vedanta tradition (<i><a href="/wiki/Achintya_Bheda_Abheda" title="Achintya Bheda Abheda">achintya bheda abheda</a></i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[note 34]</a></sup> in part a foundation of the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">ISKCON</a> (Hare Krishna) interpretation of the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekstrand175_339-1"><a href="#cite_note-ekstrand175-339">[301]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Other classical commentators include
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_(philosopher)" title="Bhāskara (philosopher)">Bhāskara</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 900 CE</span>) disagreed with Adi Shankara, wrote his own commentary on both <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> and <i>Brahma Sutras</i> in the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bhedābheda</i></span> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p37_337-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p37-337">[299]</a></sup> According to Bhaskara, the Gita is essentially Advaita, but not quite exactly, suggesting that "the <i>Atman</i> (Self) of all beings are like waves in the ocean that is Brahman". Bhaskara also disagreed with Shankara's formulation of the <i>Maya</i> doctrine, stating that prakriti, atman and Brahman are all metaphysically real.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p37_337-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p37-337">[299]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yamunacharya" title="Yamunacharya">Yamunacharya</a>, Ramanuja's teacher, summarised the teachings of the <i>Gita</i> in his <i>Gitartha sangraham</i>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nimbarka" title="Nimbarka">Nimbarka</a> (1162 CE) followed Bhaskara, but it is unclear if he ever wrote a commentary. The commentary <i>Gita tattva prakashika</i> is generally attributed to a student named Kesava Bhatta in his tradition, written in a hybrid monist-dualist manner, which states that <i>Dasasloki</i>—possibly authored by Nimbarka—teaches the essence of the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p37_337-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p37-337">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_338-1"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-338">[300]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dnyaneshwar" title="Dnyaneshwar">Dnyaneshwar's</a> (1290 CE)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dnyandevfull_322-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dnyandevfull-322">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[303]</a></sup> commentary <i><a href="/wiki/Dnyaneshwari" title="Dnyaneshwari">Dnyaneshwari</a></i> (<abbr title="also known as">a.k.a.</abbr> <i>Jnaneshwari</i> or <i>Bhavarthadipika</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[304]</a></sup> is the oldest surviving literary work in the <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sircar1996_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sircar1996-344">[305]</a></sup> one of the foundations of the Varkari tradition in Maharashtra (<a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eknath" title="Eknath">Eknath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tukaram" title="Tukaram">Tukaram</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sircar1996_344-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sircar1996-344">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ranade1997_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ranade1997-345">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2011_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2011-346">[307]</a></sup> The commentary interprets the <i>Gita</i> in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[308]</a></sup> Dnyaneshwar belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Nath" title="Nath">Nath</a> yogi tradition. His commentary on the <i>Gita</i> is notable for stating that it is the devotional commitment and love with inner renunciation that matters, not the name <i>Krishna</i> or <i>Shiva</i>, either can be used interchangeably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[310]</a></sup></li>
<li>Vallabha II, a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Vallabha" title="Vallabha">Vallabha</a> (1479 CE), wrote the commentary <i>Tattvadeepika</i> in the Suddha-Advaita tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-1"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madhusudana_Saraswati" title="Madhusudana Saraswati">Madhusudana Saraswati's</a> commentary <i>Gudhartha Deepika</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-2"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hanumat's commentary <i>Paishacha-bhasya</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-3"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Anandagiri's commentary <i>Bhashya-vyakhyanam</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-4"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Nilkantha's commentary <i>Bhava-pradeeps</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-5"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Shreedhara's (1400 CE) commentary <i>Avi gita</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-6"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Dhupakara Shastri's commentary <i>Subodhini</i> is in the Advaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-7"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raghuttama_Tirtha" title="Raghuttama Tirtha">Raghuttama Tirtha's</a> (1548-1596), commentary <i>Prameyadīpikā Bhavabodha</i> is in the <a href="/wiki/Dvaita_Vedanta" title="Dvaita Vedanta">Dvaita Vedanta</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma2000266_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma2000266-350">[311]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raghavendra_Tirtha" title="Raghavendra Tirtha">Raghavendra Tirtha's</a> (1595-1671) commentary <i>Artha samgraha</i> is in the <a href="/wiki/Dvaita_Vedanta" title="Dvaita Vedanta">Dvaita Vedanta</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sadhale_319-8"><a href="#cite_note-sadhale-319">[281]</a></sup></li>
<li>Vanamali Mishra's (1650-1720) commentary <i>Gitagudharthacandrika</i> is quite similar to Madhvacharya's commentary and is in the Dvaita Vedanta tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma2000391_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma2000391-351">[312]</a></sup></li>
<li>Purushottama (1668–1781 CE), Vallabha's follower, wrote a commentary.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-era_commentaries">Modern-era commentaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Modern-era commentaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Among notable modern commentators of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> are <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Bal Gangadhar Tilak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vinoba_Bhave" title="Vinoba Bhave">Vinoba Bhave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> (who called its philosophy Anasakti Yoga), <a href="/wiki/Sri_Aurobindo" title="Sri Aurobindo">Sri Aurobindo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/B._N._K._Sharma" title="B. N. K. Sharma">B. N. K. Sharma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osho" title="Osho">Osho</a>, and Chinmayananda. Chinmayananda took a syncretistic approach to interpret the text of the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[314]</a></sup></li>
<li>Tilak wrote his commentary <i>Shrimadh Bhagavad Gita Rahasya</i> while in jail during the period 1910–1911 serving a six-year sentence imposed by the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">colonial government</a> in India for <a href="/wiki/Sedition" title="Sedition">sedition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[315]</a></sup> While noting that the <i>Gita</i> teaches possible paths to liberation, his commentary places most emphasis on Karma yoga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[316]</a></sup></li>
<li>No book was more central to Gandhi's life and thought than the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, which he referred to as his "spiritual dictionary".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor,p.88_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minor,p.88-356">[317]</a></sup> During his stay in Yeravda jail in 1929,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor,p.88_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-Minor,p.88-356">[317]</a></sup> Gandhi wrote a commentary on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> in <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>. The Gujarati manuscript was translated into English by Mahadev Desai, who provided an additional introduction and commentary. It was published with a foreword by Gandhi in 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[319]</a></sup></li>
<li>The version by <a href="/wiki/A._C._Bhaktivedanta_Swami_Prabhupada" title="A. C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada">A. C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada</a>, entitled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhagavad-Gita_as_It_Is" title="Bhagavad-Gita as It Is">Bhagavad-Gita as It Is</a>,</i> is "by far the most widely distributed of all English Gīta translations" due to the efforts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISKCON" title="ISKCON">ISKCON</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup> Its publisher, the <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bhaktivēdānta Book Trust</i></span>, estimates sales at twenty-three million copies, a figure which includes the original English edition and secondary translations into fifty-six other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-2"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup> The Prabhupada commentary interprets the <i>Gita</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavism</a> tradition of Chaitanya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-3"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup> quite similar to <a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhvacharya's</a> Dvaita Vēdanta ideology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxiv–xxvi_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009xxiv–xxvi-359">[320]</a></sup> It presents Krishna as the Supreme, a means of saving mankind from the anxiety of material existence through loving devotion. Unlike in Bengal and nearby regions of India where the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a></i> is the primary text for this tradition, the devotees of Prabhupada's ISKCON tradition have found better reception for their ideas by those curious in the West through the <i>Gita</i>, according to Richard Davis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-4"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1966, Mahārishi Mahesh Yogi published a partial translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2014_page=168_314-5"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_2014_page=168-314">[277]</a></sup></li>
<li>An abridged version with 42 verses and commentary was published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramana_Maharishi" title="Ramana Maharishi">Ramana Maharishi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[321]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Bhagavad Gita – The song of God</i>, is a commentary by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swami_Mukundananda" title="Swami Mukundananda">Swami Mukundananda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SongOfGod_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-SongOfGod-361">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paramahansa_Yogananda" title="Paramahansa Yogananda">Paramahansa Yogananda's</a> two-volume commentary on the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, called <i><a href="/wiki/God_Talks_with_Arjuna:_The_Bhagavad_Gita" title="God Talks with Arjuna: The Bhagavad Gita">God Talks with Arjuna: The Bhagavad Gita</a></i>, was released in 1995 and is available in 4 languages and an English ebook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[323]</a></sup> The book is significant in that unlike other commentaries of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, which focus on <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">karma yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">jnana yoga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">bhakti yoga</a> in relation to the <i>Gita</i>, Yogananda's work stresses the training of one's mind, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_yoga" title="Raja yoga">raja yoga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_&amp;_Francis_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_&amp;_Francis-318">[280]</a></sup> It is published by <a href="/wiki/Self-Realization_Fellowship" title="Self-Realization Fellowship">Self-Realization Fellowship</a>/<a href="/wiki/Yogoda_Satsanga_Society_of_India" title="Yogoda Satsanga Society of India">Yogoda Satsanga Society of India</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eknath_Easwaran" title="Eknath Easwaran">Eknath Easwaran's</a> commentary interprets the <i>Gita</i> for his collection of problems of daily modern life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[324]</a></sup></li>
<li>Other modern writers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swami_Parthasarathy" title="Swami Parthasarathy">Swami Parthasarathy</a> and Sādhu Vāsvāni have published their own commentaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[325]</a></sup></li>
<li>Academic commentaries include those by Jeaneane Fowler,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012-365">[326]</a></sup> Ithamar Theodor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheodor2010_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheodor2010-366">[327]</a></sup> and Robert Zaehner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaehner1969_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaehner1969-367">[328]</a></sup></li>
<li>A collection of Christian commentaries on the <i>Gita</i> has been edited by Catherine Cornille, comparing and contrasting a wide range of views on the text by theologians and religion scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[329]</a></sup></li>
<li>The book <i>The Teachings of Bhagavad Gita: Timeless Wisdom for the Modern Age</i> by Richa Tilokani offers a woman's perspective on the teachings of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> in a simplified and reader-friendly spiritual format.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[331]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dayananda_Saraswati_(Arsha_Vidya)" title="Dayananda Saraswati (Arsha Vidya)">Swami Dayananda Saraswati</a> published a four-volume Bhagavad Gītā, Home Study Course in 1998 based on transcripts from his teaching and commentary of the Bhagavad Gītā in the classroom. This was later published in 2011 in a new edition and nine volume format.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[332]</a></sup></li>
<li>Galyna Kogut and Rahul Singh published <i>An Atheist Gets the Gita</i>, a 21st-century interpretation of the 5,000-year-old text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[333]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>With its translation and study by Western scholars beginning in the early 18th century, the Bhagavad Gita gained a growing appreciation and popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup> According to the Indian historian and writer <a href="/wiki/Khushwant_Singh" title="Khushwant Singh">Khushwant Singh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>'s famous poem "<a href="/wiki/If%E2%80%94" title="If—">If—</a>" is "the essence of the message of <i>The Gita</i> in English."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[334]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Praise_and_popularity">Praise and popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Praise and popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_Bhagavad_Gita" title="Influence of Bhagavad Gita">Influence of Bhagavad Gita</a></div>
<p>The Bhagavad Gita has been highly praised, not only by prominent Indians including <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[335]</a></sup> but also by <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHijiya2000_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHijiya2000-375">[336]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herman_Hesse" title="Herman Hesse">Herman Hesse</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bansi_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-bansi-376">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[338]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BClent_Ecevit" title="Bülent Ecevit">Bülent Ecevit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>At a time when <a href="/wiki/Indian_nationalism" title="Indian nationalism">Indian nationalists</a> were seeking an indigenous basis for social and political action against colonial rule, Bhagavad Gita provided them with a rationale for their activism and fight against injustice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[340]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Bal Gangadhar Tilak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> used the text to help inspire the Indian independence movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[note 35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[note 36]</a></sup> Mahatma Gandhi expressed his love for the <i>Gita</i> in these words:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I find a solace in the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bhagavadgītā</i></span></i> that I miss even in the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>. When disappointment stares me in the face and all alone I see not one ray of light, I go back to the <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bhagavadgītā</i></span></i>. I find a verse here and a verse there and I immediately begin to smile in the midst of overwhelming tragedies – and my life has been full of external tragedies – and if they have left no visible, no indelible scar on me, I owe it all to the teaching of <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Bhagavadgītā</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[341]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>, the first Prime Minister of independent India, commented on the <i>Gita</i>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The <i>Bhagavad-Gita</i> deals essentially with the spiritual foundation of human existence. It is a call of action to meet the obligations and duties of life; yet keeping in view the spiritual nature and grander purpose of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[342]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/A._P._J._Abdul_Kalam" title="A. P. J. Abdul Kalam">A. P. J. Abdul Kalam</a>, 11th President of India, despite being a Muslim, used to read Bhagavad Gita and recite mantras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[345]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[347]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="1624" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg/250px-Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg/375px-Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg/500px-Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Detonation_T%26B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity test</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> was the first detonation of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>, which led Oppenheimer to recall verses from the Bhagavad Gita, notably being: "Now I am become Death, the destroyer of worlds".</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a>, American physicist and director of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, learned Sanskrit in 1933 and read the Bhagavad Gita in the original form, citing it later as one of the most influential books to shape his philosophy of life. Oppenheimer later recalled that, while witnessing the explosion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_nuclear_test" title="Trinity nuclear test">Trinity nuclear test</a>, he thought of verses from the Bhagavad Gita (XI,12):
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>दिवि सूर्यसहस्रस्य भवेद्युगपदुत्थिता यदि भाः सदृशी सा स्याद्भासस्तस्य महात्मनः ॥११- १२॥
If the radiance of a thousand suns were to burst at once into the sky, that would be like the splendor of the mighty one ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJungk1958201_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJungk1958201-389">[348]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Years later he would explain that another verse had also entered his head at that time:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We knew the world would not be the same. A few people laughed, a few people cried. Most people were silent. I remembered the line from the Hindu scripture, the Bhagavad Gita; <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> is trying to persuade the <a href="/wiki/Arjuna" title="Arjuna">Prince</a> that he should do his duty and, to impress him, takes on <a href="/wiki/Vishvarupa" title="Vishvarupa">his multi-armed form</a> and says, 'Now I am become Death, the destroyer of worlds.' I suppose we all thought that, one way or another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Decision_to_Drop_the_Bomb_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Decision_to_Drop_the_Bomb-390">[349]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[note 37]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Ralph Waldo Emerson, remarked the following after his first study of the <i>Gita</i>, and thereafter frequently quoted the text in his journals and letters, particularly the "work with inner renunciation" idea in his writings on man's quest for spiritual energy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1986147–149_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1986147–149-394">[352]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I owed – my friend and I owed – a magnificent day to the <i>Bhagavad Geeta</i>. It was the first of books; it was as if an empire spoke to us, nothing small or unworthy, but large, serene, consistent, the voice of an old intelligence which in another age and climate had pondered and thus disposed of the same questions which exercise us.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1986147–149_394-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1986147–149-394">[352]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_India" title="List of Prime Ministers of India">14th prime minister of India</a>, called the Bhagavad Gita "India's biggest gift to the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[353]</a></sup> Modi gave a copy of it to the then President of the United States of America, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in 2014 during his U.S. visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[354]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's largest Bhagavad Gita is in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISKCON_Temple_Delhi" title="ISKCON Temple Delhi">ISKCON Temple Delhi</a>, which is the world's largest sacred book of any religion. It weighs 800 kg and measures over 2.8 metres by 2.0 metres. It was unveiled by <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a>, the Prime Minister of India on 26 February 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-398"><a href="#cite_note-398">[356]</a></sup> On 27 February 2021, the Bhagavad Gita, was launched into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> in a <a href="/wiki/SD_card" title="SD card">SD card</a>, on a <a href="/wiki/PSLV-C51" title="PSLV-C51">PSLV-C51</a> rocket launched by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> (ISRO) from the <a href="/wiki/Satish_Dhawan_Space_Centre" title="Satish Dhawan Space Centre">Satish Dhawan Space Centre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sriharikota" title="Sriharikota">Sriharikota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[357]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticisms_and_apologetics">Criticisms and apologetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Criticisms and apologetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Ethics.2C_war_and_violence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics,_war_and_violence">Ethics, war and violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Ethics, war and violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2244" data-file-width="3034" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg/220px-Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg/330px-Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg/440px-Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fight_between_Bhisma_and_Arjun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bhisma and Arjuna in combat, illustration from <a href="/wiki/Gita_Press" title="Gita Press">Gita Press</a> <i>Mahabharata</i></div></div></div>
<p>In the <i>Gita</i>, Krishna persuades Arjuna to wage war where the enemy includes some of his own relatives and friends. In light of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">Ahimsa</a></i> (non-violence) teachings in Hindu scriptures, the <i>Gita</i> has been criticized as violating the <i>Ahimsa</i> value, or alternatively, as supporting political violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198665–73_400-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198665–73-400">[358]</a></sup> The justification of political violence when peaceful protests and all else fails, states Varma, has been a "fairly common feature of modern Indian political thought" along with the "mighty antithesis of Gandhian thought on non-violence". During the independence movement in India, Hindus considered active "burning and drowning of British goods" while technically illegal under colonial legislation, were viewed as a moral and <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">just war</a> for the sake of liberty and righteous values of the type <i>Gita</i> discusses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varma1990p232_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varma1990p232-401">[359]</a></sup> According to Paul Schaffel the influential Hindu nationalist <a href="/wiki/Vinayak_Damodar_Savarkar" title="Vinayak Damodar Savarkar">V.D. Savarkar</a> "often turned to Hindu scripture such as the Bhagavad Gita, arguing that the text justified violence against those who would harm Mother India."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[360]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Narla_Venkateswara_Rao" title="Narla Venkateswara Rao">V. R. Narla</a>, in his book length critique of the text titled <i>The Truth About the Gita,</i> criticizes the ethical teachings of the <i>Gita</i>. He argues that the ethics of the Gita are so ambiguous, that one can use it to justify any ethical position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[361]</a></sup> Narla argues that the Gita is mainly a theological argument in favor of the warrior ethos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[362]</a></sup> Narla argues that the fact that the Gita tries constantly to make Arjuna kill his kin in order to gain a petty kingdom shows it is not a pacifist work. Narla compares the Krishna of the Gita with a modern day <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorist</a>, who uses theology to excuse violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[363]</a></sup> Narla also cites <a href="/wiki/Damodar_Dharmananda_Kosambi" title="Damodar Dharmananda Kosambi">D.D. Kosambi</a> who argued that the apparent moral of the Gita is "kill your brother if duty calls, without passion; as long as you have faith in Me, all sins are forgiven...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-406"><a href="#cite_note-406">[364]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Myth and Reality</i>, the Indian historian <a href="/wiki/Damodar_Dharmananda_Kosambi" title="Damodar Dharmananda Kosambi">D.D. Kosambi</a> argued that the <i>Gita</i> was written as a religious text that could provide support for the actions of the upper castes, including the warrior caste. These sort of exhortations to battle would not have been uncommon in ancient India as it was the job of Indian bards. Kosambi writes that in the Gita, "the high god repeatedly emphasizes the great virtue of non-killing (<i>ahimsa</i>), yet the entire discourse is an incentive to war."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-407">[365]</a></sup> He also cites the <i>Gita</i>, which states: "if slain, you gain heaven; if victorious, the earth; so up, son of Kunti, and concentrate on fighting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_407-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-407">[365]</a></sup> Kosambi argues that the injunctions and excuses for killing found in the <i>Gita</i> are unethical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_407-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-407">[365]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian jurist and intellectual <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar" title="B. R. Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a> also saw the <i>Gita</i>'s defense of violence based on the eternity of the soul (atman) as unethical. Ambedkar wrote that "to say that killing is no killing because what is killed is the body and not the soul is an unheard of defense of murder...If Krishna were to appear as a lawyer acting for a client who is being tried for murder and pleaded the defense set out by him in the Bhagvat Gita there is not the slightest doubt that he would be sent to the lunatic asylum."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[366]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his introduction to his translation of the Gita, <a href="/wiki/Purushottama_Lal" title="Purushottama Lal">Purushottama Lal</a> argues that while Arjuna appears as a pacifist, concerned with <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">ahimsa</a>, Krishna "is the <a href="/wiki/Militarism" title="Militarism">militarist</a>" who convinces him to kill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_409-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-409">[367]</a></sup> According to Lal, Krishna makes use of a "startling" argument to convince Arjuna to kill, which Lal outlines as "the atman is eternal; only the body dies; so go ahead and kill - you will kill only the body, the atman will remain unaffected [2:19-21]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_409-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-409">[367]</a></sup> Lal states that "there could hardly be a better example of forked-tongue speciousness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_409-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-409">[367]</a></sup> Lal further argues that: "the truth of the matter surely is that no rational refutation is possible of the essential humanist position that killing is wrong...many of the answers given by Krishna appear to be evasive and occasionally sophistic. When logic fails, Krishna apparently resorts to divine magic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_409-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-409">[367]</a></sup> According to Lal, in the Gita, Krishna "stuns Arjuna with a glorious 'revelation' of psychedelic intensity." This "confidence trick" is problematic for Lal, who sees Arjuna's plight as a "painful and honest problem that Krishna should have faced on its own terms, painfully and honesty, and did not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_409-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-409">[367]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mahatma Gandhi credited his commitment for <i>ahimsa</i> to the <i>Gita</i>. For Gandhi, the <i>Gita</i> is teaching that people should fight for justice and righteous values, that they should never meekly suffer injustice to avoid a war. According to the Indologist Ananya Vajpeyi, the <i>Gita</i> does not elaborate on the means or stages of war, nor on <i>ahimsa</i>, except for stating that "<i>ahimsa</i> is virtuous and characterizes an awakened, steadfast, ethical man" in verses such as 13.7–10 and 16.1–5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vajpeyi2012p66_410-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vajpeyi2012p66-410">[368]</a></sup> For Gandhi, states Vajpeyi, <i>ahimsa</i> is the "relationship between self and other" while he and his fellow Indians battled against the colonial rule. Gandhian ahimsa is in fact "the essence of the entire <i>Gita</i>", according to Vajpeyi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vajpeyi2012p66_410-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vajpeyi2012p66-410">[368]</a></sup> The teachings of the <i>Gita</i> on <i>ahimsa</i> are ambiguous, states Arvind Sharma, and this is best exemplified by the fact that Nathuram Godse stated the <i>Gita</i> as his inspiration to do his <i>dharma</i> after he <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Mahatma Gandhi">assassinated Mahatma Gandhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1986xiv–xv-134">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Merton" title="Thomas Merton">Thomas Merton</a>, the Trappist monk and author of books on Zen Buddhism, concurs with Gandhi and states that the <i>Gita</i> is not teaching violence nor propounding a "make war" ideology. Instead, it is teaching peace and discussing one's duty to examine what is right and then act with pure intentions, when one's faces difficult and repugnant choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1986149–150_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1986149–150-411">[369]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Varna_and_svadharma">Varna and svadharma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Varna and svadharma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Gita</i> has been cited and criticized as a Hindu text that supports <i>varna-dharma</i> and the caste system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-412"><a href="#cite_note-412">[370]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-413"><a href="#cite_note-413">[371]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822-414">[372]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar" title="B. R. Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a>, born in a <a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">Dalit</a> family and the principal architect of the Constitution of India, criticized the text for its stance on caste and for "defending certain dogmas of religion on philosophical grounds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822_414-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822-414">[372]</a></sup> According to Jimmy Klausen, Ambedkar in his essay <i>Krishna and his Gita</i> stated that the <i>Gita</i> was a "tool" of Brahmanical Hinduism and for its latter-day saints such as Mahatma Gandhi and <a href="/wiki/Bal_Gangadhar_Tilak" title="Bal Gangadhar Tilak">Lokmanya Tilak</a>. To Ambedkar, states Klausen, it is a text of "mostly barbaric, religious particularisms" offering "a defence of the <i>kshatriya</i> duty to make war and kill, the assertion that <i>varna</i> derives from birth rather than worth or aptitude, and the injunction to perform <i>karma</i>" neither perfunctorily nor egotistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-415"><a href="#cite_note-415">[373]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Myth and Reality</i>, <a href="/wiki/Damodar_Dharmananda_Kosambi" title="Damodar Dharmananda Kosambi">D.D. Kosambi</a> argued that "practically anything can be read into the Gita by a determined person, without denying the validity of a class system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-416">[374]</a></sup> Kosambi argued that the <i>Gita</i> was a scripture that supported the superiority of the higher varnas while seeing all other varnas as "defiled by their very birth, though they may in after-life be freed by their faith in the god who degrades them so casually in this one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_416-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-416">[374]</a></sup> He quotes the Gita which states that Krishna says "The four-caste (class) division has been created by Me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_416-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-416">[374]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p2322_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p2322-417">[375]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Narla_Venkateswara_Rao" title="Narla Venkateswara Rao">V. R. Narla</a> also argues that the <i>Gita</i> states that God created the caste (varna) system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-418">[376]</a></sup> Narla also critiques the Gita for stating that those who are not kshatriyas or brahmins are "born from sinful wombs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_418-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-418">[376]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Gita</i> presents its teaching in the context of a war where the warrior Arjuna is in inner crisis about whether he should renounce and abandon the battlefield, or fight and kill the enemy (which includes many relatives and friends of his). He is advised by Krishna to do his <i>sva-dharma</i>, a term that has been variously interpreted. According to the Indologist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hacker_(Indologist)" title="Paul Hacker (Indologist)">Paul Hacker</a>, the contextual meaning in the <i>Gita</i> is the "dharma of a particular <a href="/wiki/Varna_(Hinduism)" title="Varna (Hinduism)">varna</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995264_419-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995264-419">[377]</a></sup> In this case, Arjuna is part of the warrior (<i><a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">kshatriya</a></i>) varna (social class), so Krishna is telling Arjuna to do what warrior social class must do by virtue of his belonging to that class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995264_419-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995264-419">[377]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neo-Hindus such as <a href="/wiki/Bankim_Chandra_Chatterjee" title="Bankim Chandra Chatterjee">Bankim Chandra Chatterjee</a>, states Hacker, have preferred to not translate it in those terms, or "dharma" as religion, but leave <i>Gita's</i> message as "everyone must follow his <i>sva-dharma</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262_420-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262-420">[378]</a></sup> According to Chatterjee, the Hindus already understand the meaning of that term. To render it in English for non-Hindus for its better understanding, one must ask <i>what is the sva-dharma for the non-Hindus</i>? The Lord, states Chatterjee, created millions and millions of people, and he did not ordain <i>dharma</i> only for Indians [Hindus] and "make all the others dharma-less", for "are not the non-Hindus also his children"? According to Chatterjee, the Krishna's religion of <i>Gita</i> is "not so narrow-minded".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262_420-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262-420">[378]</a></sup> This argument, states Hacker, is an attempt to "universalize Hinduism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262_420-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995261–262-420">[378]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nadkarni and Zelliot present the opposite view, citing early Bhakti saints of the Krishna-tradition such as the 13th-century saint <a href="/wiki/Dnyaneshwar" title="Dnyaneshwar">Dnyaneshwar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822–23_421-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaitra201822–23-421">[379]</a></sup> According to Dnyaneshwar, the <i>Gita</i> starts off with the discussion of <i>sva-dharma</i> in Arjuna's context but ultimately shows that caste differences are not important. For Dnyaneshwar, people err when they see themselves distinct from each other and Krishna, and these distinctions vanish as soon as they accept, understand and enter with love unto Krishna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-422"><a href="#cite_note-422">[380]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016232–233_423-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016232–233-423">[381]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>, <i>sva-dharma</i> in the <i>Gita</i> does not mean "caste duty", rather it means the duty that comes with one's life situation (mother, father, husband, wife) or profession (soldier, judge, teacher, doctor). For Vivekananda, the <i>Gita</i> was an egalitarian scripture that rejected caste and other hierarchies because of its verses such as 13.27—28, which states "He who sees the Supreme Lord dwelling equally in all beings, the Imperishable in things that perish, he sees verily. For seeing the Lord as the same everywhere present, he does not destroy the Self by the Self, and thus he goes to the highest goal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201696–97_424-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni201696–97-424">[382]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-427"><a href="#cite_note-427">[note 38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sri_Aurobindo" title="Sri Aurobindo">Aurobindo</a> modernises the concept of <i>dharma</i> by internalising it, away from the social order and its duties towards one's personal capacities, which leads to a radical individualism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266_428-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266-428">[385]</a></sup> "finding the fulfilment of the purpose of existence in the individual alone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266_428-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266-428">[385]</a></sup> He deduced from the <i>Gita</i> the doctrine that "the functions of a man ought to be determined by his natural turn, gift, and capacities",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266_428-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266-428">[385]</a></sup> that the individual should "develop freely"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266_428-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266-428">[385]</a></sup> and thereby would be best able to serve society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266_428-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995266-428">[385]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gandhi's view differed from Aurobindo's view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995267_429-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995267-429">[386]</a></sup> He recognised in the concept of <i>sva-dharma</i> his idea of <i>svadeshi</i> (sometimes spelled <i>swadeshi</i>), the idea that "man owes his service above all to those who are nearest to him by birth and situation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995267_429-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995267-429">[386]</a></sup> To him, <i>svadeshi</i> was "<i>sva-dharma</i> applied to one's immediate environment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995268_430-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackerHalbfass1995268-430">[387]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Suthren_Hirst" title="Jacqueline Suthren Hirst">Jacqueline Hirst</a>, the universalist neo-Hindu interpretations of <i>dharma</i> in the <i>Gita</i> are modernist readings, though any study of pre-modern distant foreign cultures is inherently subject to suspicions about "control of knowledge" and bias on the various sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hirst1997p502_431-0"><a href="#cite_note-hirst1997p502-431">[388]</a></sup> Hindus have their own understanding of <i>dharma</i> that goes much beyond the <i>Gita</i> or any particular Hindu text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hirst1997p502_431-1"><a href="#cite_note-hirst1997p502-431">[388]</a></sup> Further, states Hirst, the <i>Gita</i> should be seen as a "unitary text" in its entirety rather than a particular verse analyzed separately or out of context. Krishna is presented as a teacher who "drives Arjuna and the reader beyond initial preconceptions". The <i>Gita</i> is a cohesively knit pedagogic text, not a list of norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-432"><a href="#cite_note-432">[389]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-Hinduism">Modern-Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Modern-Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Novel interpretations of the <i>Gita</i>, along with apologetics on it, have been a part of the modern era revisionism and renewal movements within Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198634–35,_131–133,_147–149_433-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198634–35,_131–133,_147–149-433">[390]</a></sup> Bankim Chandra Chatterji, the author of <i><a href="/wiki/Vande_Mataram" title="Vande Mataram">Vande Mataram</a></i> – the national song of India, challenged orientalist literature on Hinduism and offered his interpretations of the <i>Gita</i>, states Ajit Ray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198634–43_434-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198634–43-434">[391]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHacker1958261_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHacker1958261-236">[217]</a></sup> Bal Gangadhar Tilak interpreted the <i>karma yoga</i> teachings in <i>Gita</i> as a "doctrine of liberation" taught by Hinduism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198644–57_435-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198644–57-435">[392]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan</a> stated that the Bhagavad Gita teaches a universalist religion and the "essence of Hinduism" along with the "essence of all religions", rather than a private religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor1986154,_161–163_436-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor1986154,_161–163-436">[393]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">Vivekananda</a>'s works contained numerous references to the <i>Gita</i>, such as his lectures on the four yogas – Bhakti, Jnana, Karma, and Raja.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-437"><a href="#cite_note-437">[394]</a></sup> Through the message of the <i>Gita</i>, Vivekananda sought to energise the people of India to reclaim their dormant but strong identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-438"><a href="#cite_note-438">[395]</a></sup> Aurobindo saw Bhagavad Gita as a "scripture of the future religion" and suggested that Hinduism had acquired a much wider relevance through the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-439"><a href="#cite_note-439">[396]</a></sup> Sivananda called Bhagavad Gita "the most precious jewel of Hindu literature" and suggested its introduction into the curriculum of Indian schools and colleges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-440"><a href="#cite_note-440">[397]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ronald Neufeldt, it was the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> that dedicated much attention and energy to the allegorical interpretation of the <i>Gita</i>, along with religious texts from around the world, after 1885 and given H. P. Blavatsky, Subba Rao and Anne Besant writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198611–14_441-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198611–14-441">[398]</a></sup> Their attempt was to present their "universalist religion". These late 19th-century theosophical writings called the <i>Gita</i> as a "path of true spirituality" and "teaching nothing more than the basis of every system of philosophy and scientific endeavor", triumphing over other "Samkhya paths" of Hinduism that "have degenerated into superstition and demoralized India by leading people away from practical action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinor198611–14_441-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinor198611–14-441">[398]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations">Adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philip_Glass" title="Philip Glass">Philip Glass</a> retold the story of Gandhi's early development as an activist in South Africa through the text of the <i>Gita</i> in the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Satyagraha_(opera)" title="Satyagraha (opera)">Satyagraha</a></i> (1979). The entire libretto of the opera consists of sayings from the <i>Gita</i> sung in the original Sanskrit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-442"><a href="#cite_note-442">[web 5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Douglas_J._Cuomo" title="Douglas J. Cuomo">Douglas Cuomo's</a> <i>Arjuna's dilemma</i>, the philosophical dilemma faced by Arjuna is dramatised in operatic form with a blend of Indian and Western music styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-443"><a href="#cite_note-443">[web 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1993 Sanskrit film, <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita_(film)" title="Bhagavad Gita (film)">Bhagavad Gita</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/G._V._Iyer" title="G. V. Iyer">G. V. Iyer</a> won the <a href="/wiki/40th_National_Film_Awards" title="40th National Film Awards">1993</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_(India)" title="National Film Award (India)">National Film Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Award_for_Best_Feature_Film" title="National Film Award for Best Feature Film">Best Film</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40thaward_444-0"><a href="#cite_note-40thaward-444">[web 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40thawardPDF_445-0"><a href="#cite_note-40thawardPDF-445">[web 8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1995 novel by Steven Pressfield, and its adaptation as the 2000 golf movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Bagger_Vance" title="The Legend of Bagger Vance">The Legend of Bagger Vance</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Redford" title="Robert Redford">Robert Redford</a> has parallels to the Bhagavad Gita, according to Steven J. Rosen. Steven Pressfield acknowledges that the <i>Gita</i> was his inspiration, the golfer character in his novel is Arjuna, the caddie is Krishna, states Rosen. The movie, however, uses the plot but glosses over the teachings unlike in the novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-446"><a href="#cite_note-446">[399]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ashtavakra_Gita" title="Ashtavakra Gita">Ashtavakra Gita</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avadhuta_Gita" title="Avadhuta Gita">Avadhuta Gita</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Devi_Gita" title="Devi Gita">Devi Gita</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ganesha_Purana#The_Ganesha_Gita" title="Ganesha Purana">The Ganesha Gita</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Self-consciousness_(Vedanta)" title="Self-consciousness (Vedanta)">Self-consciousness (Vedanta)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uddhava#Uddhava_Gita" title="Uddhava">Uddhava Gita</a></li>
