<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"God" here denotes <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavan" title="Bhagavan">Bhagavan</a></i> i.e, <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-columns-2">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Krishna states that the body is impermanent and dies, never the immortal Self, the latter being either reborn or achieving <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i> for those who have understood the true spiritual path he teaches in the <i>Gita</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Bhagavad Gita also integrates <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">transcendentalism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup> or spiritual<a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaju1992211_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaju1992211-14">[11]</a></sup> and identifies a God of personal characteristics with the <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-BG_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-EB-BG-4">[web 1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This legend is depicted with Ganesha (Vinayaka) iconography in Hindu temples where he is shown with a broken right tusk and his right arm holds the broken tusk as if it was a stylus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rao1985_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rao1985-31">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The debate about the relationship between the <i>Gita</i> and the <i>Mahabharata</i> is historic, in part the basis for chronologically placing the <i>Gita</i> and its authorship. The Indologist Franklin Edgerton was among the early scholars and a translator of the <i>Gita</i> who believed that the <i>Gita</i> was a later composition that was inserted into the epic, at a much later date, by a creative poet of great intellectual power intimately aware of emotional and spiritual aspects of human existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edgerton1952_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-edgerton1952-40">[35]</a></sup> Edgerton's primary argument was that it makes no sense that two massive armies facing each other on a battlefield will wait for two individuals to have a lengthy dialogue. Further, he states that the <i>Mahabharata</i> has numerous such interpolations and inserting the <i>Gita</i> would not be unusual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edgerton1952_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-edgerton1952-40">[35]</a></sup> In contrast, the Indologist James Fitzgerald states, in a manner similar to van Buitenen, that the Bhagavad Gita is the centerpiece and essential to the ideological continuity in the <i>Mahabharata</i>, and the entire epic builds up to the fundamental dharma questions in the <i>Gita</i>. This text, states Fitzgerald, must have been integral to the earliest version of the epic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Basham, passionately theistic verses are found, for example, in chapters 4, 7, 9, 10, 11, 14.1–6 with 14.29, 15, 18.54–78; while more philosophical verses with one or two verses where Krishna identifies himself as the highest god are found, for example, in chapters 2.38–72, 3, 5, 6, 8, 13 and 14.7–25, 16, 17 and 18.1–53. Further, states Basham, the verses that discuss <i>Gita's</i> "motiveless action" doctrine was probably authored by someone else and these constitute the most important ethical teaching of the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basham1991p85_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-basham1991p85-44">[38]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Indologist and Sanskrit literature scholar <a href="/wiki/Moriz_Winternitz" title="Moriz Winternitz">Moriz Winternitz</a>, the founder of the early Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Sautr%C4%81ntika" title="Sautrāntika">Sautrāntika</a> school named Kumaralata (1st century CE) mentions both <i>Mahabharata</i> and <i>Ramayana</i>, along with early Indian history on writing, art and painting, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_Buddhist_literature" title="Sanskrit Buddhist literature">Kalpanamanditika</a></i> text. Fragments of this early text have survived into the modern era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Indologist <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Lamotte" title="Étienne Lamotte">Étienne Lamotte</a> used a similar analysis to conclude that the <i>Gita</i> in its current form likely underwent one redaction that occurred in the 3rd or 2nd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[46]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They state that the authors of the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> must have seen the appeal of the soteriologies found in "the heterodox traditions of Buddhism and Jainism" as well as those found in " the orthodox Hindu traditions of Samkhya and Yoga". The <i>Gita</i> attempts to present a harmonious, universalist answer, state Deutsch and Dalvi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliot_DeutschRohit_Dalvi200461–62-15">[12]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is called the doctrine of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nishkam_Karma" title="Nishkam Karma">nishakama karma</a></i> in Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Framarin_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Framarin-88">[78]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An alternate way to describe the poetic structure of <i>Gita</i>, according to Sargeant, is that it consists of "four lines of eight syllables each", similar to one found in Longfellow's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Hiawatha" title="The Song of Hiawatha">Hiawatha</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant20098_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant20098-111">[100]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the epic <i>Mahabharata</i>, after <a href="/wiki/Sanjaya" title="Sanjaya">Sanjaya</a>—counsellor of the <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru</a> king <a href="/wiki/Dhritarashtra" title="Dhritarashtra">Dhritarashtra</a>—returns from the battlefield to announce the death of <a href="/wiki/Bhishma" title="Bhishma">Bhishma</a>, he begins recounting the details of the <i>Mahabharata</i> war. <i>Bhagavad Gita</i> is a part of this recollection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[103]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some editions include the <i><a href="/wiki/Gita_Dhyanam" title="Gita Dhyanam">Gita Dhyanam</a></i> consisting of 9 verses. The <i>Gita Dhyanam</i> is not a part of the original <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, but some modern era versions insert it as a prefix to the <i>Gītā</i>. The verses of the <i>Gita Dhyanam</i> (also called <i>Gītā Dhyāna</i> or <i>Dhyāna Ślokas</i>) offer salutations to a variety of sacred scriptures, figures, and entities, characterise the relationship of the <i>Gītā</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>, and affirm the power of divine assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chinmaya98_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-chinmaya98-128">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[114]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avatara" title="Avatara">avatara</a> concept found in the Vaishnavism tradition of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEaswaran2007111–122-5">[3]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For alternate worded translations, see Radhakrishnan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERadhakrishnan1993187_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERadhakrishnan1993187-149">[132]</a></sup> Miller,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller198663_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller198663-150">[133]</a></sup> Sargeant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargeant2009272_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargeant2009272-151">[134]</a></sup> Edgerton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[135]</a></sup> Flood &amp; Martin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[136]</a></sup> and others.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-198">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This contrasts with a few competing schools of Indian religions which denied the concept of Self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[179]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-250"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-250">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNikhilanandaHocking2006">Nikhilananda &amp; Hocking 2006</a>, p. 2 "Arjuna represents the individual Self, and Sri Krishna the Supreme Self dwelling in every heart. Arjuna's chariot is the body. The blind king Dhritarashtra is the mind under the spell of ignorance, and his hundred sons are man's numerous evil tendencies. The battle, a perennial one, is between the power of good and the power of evil. The warrior who listens to the advice of the Lord speaking from within will triumph in this battle and attain the Highest Good."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-257"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-257">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aurobindo writes, "... That is a view which the general character and the actual language of the epic does not justify and, if pressed, would turn the straightforward philosophical language of the <i>Gita</i> into a constant, laborious and somewhat puerile mystification ... the <i>Gita</i> is written in plain terms and professes to solve the great ethical and spiritual difficulties which the life of man raises, and it will not do to go behind this plain language and thought and wrest them to the service of our fancy. But there is this much of truth in the view, that the setting of the doctrine though not symbolical, is certainly typical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THD_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-THD-256">[236]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-270"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-270">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other parallelism include verse 10.21 of Gita replicating the structure of verse 1.2.5 of the Shatapatha Brahmana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson2002157_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson2002157-269">[248]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-284"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-284">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sanskrit scholar <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Stoler_Miller" title="Barbara Stoler Miller">Barbara Stoler Miller</a> produced a translation in 1986 intended to emphasise the poem's influence and current context within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Literature" title="English Literature">English Literature</a>, especially the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T.S._Eliot" title="T.S. Eliot">T.S. Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[258]</a></sup> The translation was praised by scholars as well as literary critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[260]</a></sup> Similarly, the Hinduism scholar Jeaneane Fowler's translation and student text has been praised for its comprehensive introduction, quality of translation, and commentary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[261]</a></sup></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-317"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-317">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Teachings of <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">International Society for Krishna Consciousness</a> (ISKCON), a <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnava</a> religious organisation which spread rapidly in North America in the 1970s and 1980s, are based on a translation of the <i>Gita</i> called <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavad-G%C4%ABt%C4%81_As_It_Is" title="Bhagavad-Gītā As It Is">Bhagavad-Gītā As It Is</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.C._Bhaktivedanta_Swami_Prabhupada" title="A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada">A.C. Bhaktivedanta Swami Prabhupada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[278]</a></sup> These teachings are also illustrated in the <a href="/wiki/Diorama" title="Diorama">dioramas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad-gita_Museum" title="Bhagavad-gita Museum">Bhagavad-gita Museum</a> in Los Angeles, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[279]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-340"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-340">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Edwin Bryant and Maria Ekstrand, this school incorporates and integrates aspects of "qualified monism, dualism, monistic dualism, and pure nondualism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekstrand175_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-ekstrand175-339">[301]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-380"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-380">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For B.G. Tilak and Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi as notable commentators see: <a href="#CITEREFGambhirananda1997">Gambhirananda 1997</a>, p. xix</span>

<li id="cite_note-393"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-393">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Oppenheimer spoke these words in the television documentary <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.atomicarchive.com/Movies/Movie8.shtml" rel="nofollow">The Decision to Drop the Bomb</a></i> (1965).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Decision_to_Drop_the_Bomb_390-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Decision_to_Drop_the_Bomb-390">[349]</a></sup> Oppenheimer read the original text in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, "<span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">kālo'smi lokakṣayakṛtpravṛddho lokānsamāhartumiha pravṛttaḥ</i></span>" (XI,32), which he translated as "I am become Death, the destroyer of worlds". In the literature, the quote usually appears in the form <i>shatterer</i> of worlds, because this was the form in which it first appeared in print, in <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a> on 8 November 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[350]</a></sup> It later appeared in Robert Jungk's <i>Brighter than a Thousand Suns: A Personal History of the Atomic Scientists</i> (1958),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJungk1958201_389-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJungk1958201-389">[348]</a></sup> which was based on an interview with Oppenheimer. See Hijiya, <i>The Gita of Robert Oppenheimer</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHijiya2000123–124_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHijiya2000123–124-392">[351]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-427"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-427">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This view in the <i>Gita</i> of the unity and equality in the essence of all individual beings as the hallmark of a spiritually liberated, wise person is also found in the classical and modern commentaries on <i>Gita</i> verses 5.18, 6.29, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2012100,_118–119_425-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2012100,_118–119-425">[383]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016233–234_426-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.V._Nadkarni2016233–234-426">[384]</a></sup> Scholars have contested Kosambi's criticism of the <i>Gita</i> based on its various sections on karma yoga, bhakti yoga and jnana yoga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadkarni2016p2322_417-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nadkarni2016p2322-417">[375]</a></sup></span>
</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bhagavad_Gita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li id="cite_note-minor1982lli-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-minor1982lli_105-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMinor1982">Minor 1982</a>, pp. l–li, Quote: "The current text of the Bhagavad Gita is well-preserved with relatively few variant readings and none quite serious. This is especially remarkable in the light of the numerous variants for the remainder of the Mahabharata, some of which are quite serious. Secondary insertions are found in individual manuscripts of the Gita, but these are clearly secondary. The number of stanzas in the Gita is 700, a number confirmed by Shankara, and possibly deliberately chosen in order to prevent interpolations."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Weinstein-India-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Weinstein-India_161-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Max Bernhard Weinsten, <i>Welt- und Lebensanschauungen, Hervorgegangen aus Religion, Philosophie und Naturerkenntnis</i> ("World and Life Views, Emerging From Religion, Philosophy and Perception of Nature") (1910), p. 213: "Wir werden später sehen, daß die Indier auch den Pandeismus gelehrt haben. Der letzte Zustand besteht in dieser Lehre im Eingehen in die betreffende Gottheit, Brahma oder Wischnu. So sagt in der Bhagavad-Gîtâ Krishna-Wischnu, nach vielen Lehren über ein vollkommenes Dasein."</span>

Motilal Banarsidass. pp. 409–410. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-208-1468-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-208-1468-4"><bdi>978-81-208-1468-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sixty+Upani%E1%B9%A3ads+of+the+Veda&amp;rft.pages=409-410&amp;rft.pub=Motilal+Banarsidass&amp;rft.date=1980&amp;rft.isbn=978-81-208-1468-4&amp;rft.au=Paul+Deussen&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D8mSpQo9q-tIC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABhagavad+Gita"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-195">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><b>[a]</b> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141230210157/http://www.oxforddictionaries.com/us/definition/american_english/atman" rel="nofollow">Atman</a>, Oxford Dictionaries, Oxford University Press (2012), <b>Quote</b>: "1. real self of the individual; 2. a person's Self";<br/><b>[b]</b> John Bowker (2000), The Concise Oxford Dictionary of World Religions, Oxford University Press, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-280094-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-280094-7">978-0-19-280094-7</a>, See entry for Atman;<br/><b>[c]</b> WJ Johnson (2009), <a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q55879169" title="d:Q55879169">A Dictionary of Hinduism</a>, Oxford University Press, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-861025-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-861025-0">978-0-19-861025-0</a>, See entry for Atman (self). <span style="font-size:0.95em; font-size:95%; color:#555">(subscription or <a class="external text" href="https://www.oxforddnb.com/help/subscribe#public" rel="nofollow">UK public library membership</a> required)</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John C. Plott et al. (2000), Global History of Philosophy: The Axial Age, Volume 1, Motilal Banarsidass, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-208-0158-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-208-0158-5">978-81-208-0158-5</a>, p. 63, Quote: "The Buddhist schools reject any Ātman concept. As we have already observed, this is the basic and ineradicable distinction between Hinduism and Buddhism".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-197">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><b>[a]</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/anatta" rel="nofollow">Anatta</a>, Encyclopædia Britannica (2013), <b>Quote:</b> "Anatta in Buddhism, the doctrine that there is in humans no permanent, underlying Self. The concept of anatta, or anatman, is a departure from the Hindu belief in atman ("the self").";<br/><b>[b]</b> Steven Collins (1994), Religion and Practical Reason (Editors: Frank Reynolds, David Tracy), State Univ of New York Press, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7914-2217-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7914-2217-5">978-0-7914-2217-5</a>, p. 64; <b>Quote:</b> "Central to Buddhist soteriology is the doctrine of not-self (Pali: anattā, Sanskrit: anātman, the opposed doctrine of ātman is central to Brahmanical thought). Put very briefly, this is the [Buddhist] doctrine that human beings have no soul, no self, no unchanging essence.";<br/><b>[c]</b> Edward Roer (Translator), <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=3uwDAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA2" rel="nofollow">Shankara's Introduction</a></i>, p. 2, at <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a> to <i>Brihad Aranyaka Upanishad</i>, pp. 2–4;<br/><b>[d]</b> KN Jayatilleke (2010), Early Buddhist Theory of Knowledge, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-208-0619-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-208-0619-1">978-81-208-0619-1</a>, pp. 246–249, from note 385 onwards;<br/><b>[e]</b> Bruno Nagel (2000), Roy Perrett (editor), Philosophy of Religion: Indian Philosophy, Routledge, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8153-3611-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8153-3611-2">978-0-8153-3611-2</a>, p. 33, <b>Quote:</b> "The dispute with Buddhists, who do not accept an imperishable Self, gives the Atman schools [Vedanta, Kashmir Shaivism] a chance to articulate the intellectual aspects of their way to meditative liberation".</span>
